=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Generally thematic worker placement game about building uranium and/or plutonium bombs.

It's a typical worker placement game, with the following 2 exceptions: 1) as an action, you need to recall back your workers (similar to Tournay, you don't get them back automatically, so this creates some occasional interesting decisions), and 2) you get additional extra placements on your own personal board where you will have built buildings, allowing for potential sequential multiple placements.  You have options such as acquiring more workers (there are some with minor special abilities such as engineers and scientists), acquiring money (needed to build bombs, buy buildings, acquire resources such as yellowcake/uranium/plutonium), acquiring plutonium, uranium, yellowcake, acquiring bomb plans, building up your military strength, attacking other players, spying on them (using their buildings), and constructing bombs.  All of the points in the game are through the construction of bombs.

The buildings are typically upgrades of all of the available actions on the board.  Money is very tight and essential.  Workers seem plentiful, but more is always better and you can also acquire more than your allotment by hiring temporary contract workers.  You can play an arms race and be aggressive or not (games play very differently depending on the style of players involved or the synergy of the buildings that a player has).  The game ends "sudden death" with the first player to meet the point scoring requirement (points are ONLY scored through bomb building, which is multi-step process).  Nice artwork and very clear rulebook.  A very solid first design.

Own the [boardgame=128883]Second Stage[/boardgame] and [boardgame=108421]Nations[/boardgame] expansions.

RATING: 7
A very intuitive medium weight worker placement game.

I like the mechanics used with the worker placement in this game. I'm not very experienced in the genre, but I'm aware of the new things introduced here and appreciate them.

I like the play time for this at 2 hours. It doesn't overstay and I don't feel like it ended too quickly. It starts to wrap just as the engine is cranked up. The rate of escalation in the gameplay is a rewarding feeling. Very well designed. We've only played it at 2 player, and I imagine it will require some different strategies with a higher player count given the plutonium vs uranium bomb cards as well as reduced bomb end game target.

Another game with 'take that' actions, which I am a fan of. It keeps me engaged in what the other player(s) are doing and keeps me on guard. I've been the victim more often than not, but I enjoy it. There is a risk/reward for it, and it feels warranted in this game both strategically and thematically.

I've yet to win this game, and I still love it.

RATING: 8.3
It's certainly a very good game; but there's nothing at all innovative about it. If you've played any worker placement/engine building game, then you've already played The Manhattan Project with a different theme. Yes, people talk about how vicious and cutthroat it can be with the airstrikes and espionage elements; but it is so easy to either avoid such things or quickly recover from them, that the game ain't anywhere near as cutthroat as you might believe. Carson City has a far better version of "take that" in a worker-placement game. (Incidentally, if you dislike cutthroat games, The Manhattan Project shouldn't bother you too much.)

I have two significant complaints: one, it's the most fiddly game I have ever played; and two, it was cheaply produced. Too much material was crammed into a small space. The best example of this is the uranium and plutonium tracks: it would have made a LOT more sense to have uranium and plutonium cubes.

RATING: 4
As a disclaimer, I've only played this once.  But from that one play, I don't see the appeal.  The game mechanics are pretty much terrible across the board - while they are interesting enough, the whole pick-up-and-return worker mechanic means that in the early game you have good decisions but in the late game you are just dropping everything on your player board, taking some vaguely useful action on the main board, and then waiting for two more go-rounds so you can act again.  There's player interaction, sure, but it's pretty much "hope you don't get attacked first, and hope you don't attack someone first because if you do your fighter level is down and now you get blown up too".  And combat is the only way the game even pretends to let you slow down a leader.

Bleh.

RATING: 7
Manhattan Project might be yet another worker placement game, but at least it scores for an original theme: building nuclear bombs.  It has reasonable fidelity to that theme as well.  There are no mechanics that leave you wondering what on earth they represent.  It is also  state of the worker placement art, with three varieties of worker, a whole turn spent retrieving placed workers, and even a common supply of contractors (one use workers in each of the three varieties).

Manhattan Project is yet another engine-building game.  Yet it throws spanners in the works of your perfect plan by introducing espionage (someone else may be using that facility that you carefully saved up for) and bombing raids that can put your engine out of action. While I'm generally in favour of any form of player interaction, I do wonder about the air war in this game. In the two games I have played we have all been wary of it.  However, I suspect the potential for setting off a chain of air attacks that bombs everywhere flat and significantly lengthens a game that's already long enough.

Another concern relates to strategic variation.  You always want a large workforce, a source of yellow cake (raw nuclear material) and the capacity to convert that into uranium and plutonium. You always want to reach the vp target by building as few bombs as possible.

The fifties cartoony artwork is a delight and production quality is also really good.  The rules were an easy read and left us with no questions.

RATING: 6
This is an interesting case of a worker placement game having too many workers for my tastes.  And I never thought I would feel that way about a worker placement game since I do LOVE getting extra workers in all worker placement games. 

The idea here seems to be to enjoy powering up long combos on your turn, with your unlimited placements on your buildings, such that essentially, each player trusts that the other players are using their 25 workers properly on all their buildings.  Way too many workers to have to watch for my tastes!  I trust you, go ahead and do all that.  Then, at some point, somebody gets jealous of all the combos that another player is reeling off with their buildings and bombs them or takes them over via espionage.  

From a game design point of view, perhaps bombing and espionage were incorporated to allow unlucky players to catch up to a player who, through the luck of the card draw, happened to be able to purchase an extremely powerful building early in game.  Does bombing and espionage, however, feel like a fun game mechanic to play with?  Not so much for me, but then again, I am not a war gamer.

Bottom line, I have no desire to play this game again, though I do respect the design and all the thematic work that went into the game, so gotta give it a 6 at best.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The best worker placement game so far. 
The three best features in this game are:
1) No 1st player space (that seems to be mandatory in most other worker placement games). Always a problem if you happened to sit to the right of the player that is very keen on being 1st. That it should matter in what order you sit around a table is ludicrous.
2) Damage other players building if their engine is going at full speed and yours is still starting up
3) Use spies to use other players building instead of buying your own.

Highly recommended!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Manhattan Project combines worker-placement and engine-building mechanics to produce a unique euro where players race to obtain victory points. But it's also a euro that is quite interactive as players can use and bomb your own facilities. And because of this interactive nature, this is a game where you don't necessarily want the best cards (other players may bomb them) and one where you must carefully time when you return your own workers (other players may use your cards and disrupt your engine). 

Overall, this is one of those games that I find fascinating but not necessary one I crave to play. At higher player counts the game suffers from meta-gaming and king-making; and if you find yourself a victim to this popularity contest, it can be very difficult to recover. With two-players, king-making/meta-gaming isn't a concern but the game can be absolutely devastating if a player makes a mistake and it often develops in a wonky way. Truth be told, the devastating possibilities don't bother me since the game has few random elements and they can be mitigated. But the two-player experience simply doesn't flow in a satisfactory way.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The components are nice (even if the cards are bit too small), the artwork is nice and the theme is certainly unique. The game is easy to understand and flows very well. It does suffer from a dominate strategy - because it is a worker placement (i.e. action selection) with easily obtained worker with no subsequent upkeep costs, the key to the game to always get more workers. Always. Get a university ASAP and always first play a guy on the worker portion of the board. Anybody who falls behind here will surely loose the game.

RATING: 6
A játék első felében csupa pozitív élményem volt, viccesek az emberkék meg a pogácsák, ötletes a kémkedés és a bábuk menedzselése, a játék nagyon tematikus, nem az a "tucat-worker placement", öröm vele játszani!... de a közepétől kezdett kicsit mechanikus lenni, és van két dolog, ami megöli szerintem: az egyik a győzelem bekövetkezésének módja, ami bár történelem hű, de... mégse tudom tolerálni. Fél órás játéknál elmenne, de 2 órásnál nem. Ugyanis bármelyik pillanatban bekövetkezhet, és kiegyenlített partinál szinte lehetetlen megjósolni, hogy épp ki csinálja meg a győztes robbantást, hacsak nincs nagyon jó memóriája valakinek, bár még akkor se tudhatja tutira, kinél milyen értékű és típusú bombák vannak... Egy partijátékban ez elmenne, de ez nem az! A végső pontszám így persze mit sem mond, mert lehet, hogy 0 pontja lesz olyannak is, akinek már készen állt az 50+ pontja és csak a bejelentésre várt. (Emiatt nincs is sok értelme a pontszámoknak, csak az a lényeg, ki nyert...)
A másik ami nem tetszik, az a bombázás: bármikor nullára lehet bombázni egy gyengébb arzenálú játékost (ráadásul még konzultálni is lehet egymással ez ügyben, ami nem illik ehhez a műfajhoz szerintem). Ez is tematikus, mégsem tetszik, mert ez is egyik pillanatról a másikra történik.
Kár érte, mert csomó jó ötlet van benne, de ezektől kicsit komolytalan szerintem.
Dicsértem az elején a bábuk kezelését (mindenki egyénileg dönt, hogy mikor szedi vissza őket, nincsenek rögzített körök): ennek is van azonban egy hátránya: szélsőséges esetben előfordulhat, hogy egy közös mezőt sosem használhat valaki.
Fontos még megemlíteni, hogy a Kártyagyár kivitelezésében nagyon szépre sikerült a játék!

RATING: 6.5
Manhattan Project is a "tempo" worker placement game: on your turn, you can place as many workers as you want/can (one on the main board, the others on your onuccupied buildings or, with the espionage action, in other players' unoccupied buildins).
This is a cool concept, that has been used a few months later also by Tzol'kin (together with the awesome gears) in a much better and more developed way. I suppose there is a difference after all between a kickstarter game from a debutant, and a game tested and developed by CGE and Vlaada Chvatil...

Anyway, Manhattan Project is quite cool and is packed with interesting choices, has an awesome graphic presentation and production value (even if the workers of different colors are somewhat difficult to tell apart... and I am not colorblind!) and is full of interesting concepts.

There's a couple of things keeping it short of being great, however: some main board actions are definitely better than others (espionage!), and the cool (on paper) attack mechanic is both a welcome addition to the genre and a dangerous and not very fitting addition to the genre (in the way it is implemented). I have also some reserves on the points for the bombs: a couple of plutonium bombs after testing one are usually enough to win.

In conclusion, this is a game I would like to play again because I quite liked it and I want to refine my judgment. I had high expectations, but while many were met, something felt a little wrong and less-than-ideally developed...

RATING: 8
Pro's:

1) Great theme and aesthetic design. The worker chits are incredibly satisfying to play with due to the thickness of the cardboard.
2) Art style is perfect.
3) The ramp up quality of your nuclear bomb production machine is really well done. It feels like everything is slowly moving forward, in small increments, and then suddenly every player is one turn away from winning and it's a race to make sure you get there first. There's always that moment of Aaargh! I almost won.
4) The basic mechanic is relatively simple, and as such I have had great success teaching it to newer gamers despite the game not really being light.

Con's:

1) Espionage can sometimes feel somewhat game breaking. Though it is an excellent way to counteract some players tendency to over value bombers.
2) It can feel really mean sometimes.
3) There is a decent amount of choice presented to a player throughout the game, can cause some pretty serious AP in some players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game seemed at first to me like it had too much going on, but it only took a couple plays for me to realize that while there's a good number of options for players, the game is pretty simple and different strategies can lead to victory. In this game, you take on the roll of a world power racing to develop atomic bombs, not to use them, but to show your prowess. You do this with worker placement, by using one spot on the main board, and then as many spots in your own personal tableau as you want to use. When you run out of workers, you can instead take your turn to simply return all your workers. Some of the options you have on the main board include getting new workers, buying new buildings to add to your tableau, gaining money or yellow cake, making radioactive material (either plutonium or uranium depending on what bomb it's for), and you can even send your planes to other players boards to try to bomb their buildings to slow their progress.

There is an interesting decision to be made in this game to decide which kind of bomb (uranium and plutonium, denoted by the color red or blue) you want to make. Red bombs are a bit easier to make, but have only one score possible, where as the blue bombs can be researched in order to give more points.

This game is great for 2 or 3, and slows down and bogs down a bit too much with 4 for my taste. My only dislike for this game is the bombing other players mechanism. It can easily change the dynamic of the game to make players pick on one player, and in general I don't like this type of mechanic in engine building games, as it seems to just slow the game down unnecessarily. That being said, still a great game, and also has a good ios app version to boot.

RATING: 6
Initially I didn't like this, but after about 5 plays I think it's okay.  It seems like a worker placement game, and for the first couple rounds it is, but you quickly get a bunch of cards and a ton of workers so turns are basically- run your engine, remove your workers, run your engine etc.  There's rarely much tension of someone taking a spot where you wanted to place a worker which is usually where the player interaction takes place in most worker placement games.  But the game does have more overt attacking mechanisms to provide player interaction and they seem neat until you actually start to use them.  They are clumsy and their only real purpose is to slow down a clear leader.  But if you attack you are also slowing yourself down.  In one game I hammered espionage and picked on one player over and over, since I perceived (correctly) that she was ahead and she still won the game.  The most interesting part of the game is a sort of tempo/timing element created by taking a turn to remove your workers.  Also, the game ends very abruptly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
After 2 plays... not a bad game.  simple mechanics: on your turn, either (1) place 1 worker on the main board and/or 1 or more workers on your buildings or (2) retrieve all of your workers from everywhere, return all neutral workers on your buildings and on the main board and all other players' workers on your buildings.  The goal is to build bombs by converting uranium or plutonium that come from yellow cakes, scientists (workers) and money.  There's also a mechanic where you can bomb other people's buildings, damaging them and "spy" to place your workers on other people's buildings.  

It's not a decent resource conversion worker placement game that plays well with 2 (and I suspect a little better with more).  It's got 3 types of workers, the ability to get more of your own and temporary neutral workers.  I'd happily play it but it lacks the depth that holds my interest.  I'll play it again if Amanda wants to but probably won't ask to play it again.  I also may bring it to a Game-Day night since it's relatively quick (1 - 2 hours).  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
A fantastic worker placement game with a flavorful theme - I just love to build the bomb with those cute little workers XD

There is a lot of indirect interaction over occupying the spots on the main board and buying buildings from the shared market. The direct interaction of bombing each others buildings usually doesn't play a big role in the game as it comes with a high cost (a worker on the main board plus fighters and bombers). This often results in a cold war situation where all players build fighters and bombers to avoid being an easy target, but rarely anybody actually attacks. This fits the theme very well and drives up the tension.

RATING: 5
UPDATE: I see Kickstarter funding was successful and this is being made; I have the same mixed feelings about this as I did Eminent Domain. Manhattan Project isn't very good, but maybe it could have been with some professional development work. By making producing the game a lot less risky via Kickstarter, the designer and publisher didn't have to go the extra mile to really turn something out they had to have a high degree of confidence in, both as a quality game and as something they could sell. And so we end up with something relatively mediocre that just dilutes the marketplace of ideas.
---
Version played at BGG.con '11. Basically what you'd expect from a first-time designer looking for Kickstarter funding: clever and distinct mechanically, but definitely amateur. The gameplay is too constrained; often you have no way to make progress on your objectives because everything you need to do is blocked, and all you can do is wait for things to become unblocked. Pacing becomes very uneven and early buildings really key. Also an AP problem and limited strategic variability. Thematically odd - yes, you're mining uranium and then processing it into bombs, but it bears no relationship whatever to the historical Manhattan Project, it's really just a worker placement game in which the end result happens to be a couple nukes instead of a Church/Cathedral/Castle.

Anyway, unless it's significantly tuned over what I played, I can't recommend backing it.

RATING: 4
Manhattan Project Recipe

Workers
Buildings
Yellow cubes
Red disks
Grey disks
Money

1. Acquire as many workers as possible.

2. Acquire buildings that produce workers, money or yellow cubes.

3. Transform yellow cubes into red or grey disks.

4. Transform disks and money into victory points.

5. Pour into glass and drink.

If I didn't point out that this was the Manahattan Project, would you have been able to differentiate it from so many other worker placement efficiency engine games out there?

RATING: 7.5
Worker placement game with the Manhattan arms race theme. I had fun bombing the other players cities, however this was at the expense of my own game. Like all games of this genre you need to play a balanced strategy to win. You need defence/attack to not be vulnerable, mines to produce raw materials, factories to make the nuclear material for your bombs and universities to train your workforce. The really interesting part of the game is the use of the espionage element of the game where you can make use of other players infrastructure if you time the placement correctly.

Overall this is a solid worker placement game.

RATING: 9
Atombombát építeni cool.
Le(nematom)bombázni valaki más atombomba építő kapacitását, hogy te hamarabb megépítsd a sajátodat cool. Már ha nem veled csinálják épp. De abból is vissza lehet jönni.
A worker lehelyezős/visszavevős mechanizmus egyedi és jól működik.
Lényegében workerekből csinálunk sárga kockát (uránérc) amit újabb workerrel finomítunk plutóniummá vagy uránná, amiből csinálhatunk Pl vagy U235 bombát, utóbbik több pontot érnek de pénz is kell hozzájuk, illetve Pl bombáink is többet érhetnek ha egyet közülünk beáldozunk (tesztrobbantunk). Pénzt szintén worker-ek gyártanak, illetve repülőket csinálni (a bombázáshoz meg a légi védekezéshez) és építkezni is workerekkel (és/plusz pénzzel) tudunk. Van még kémkedés is a játékban amivel a másik játékos tábláján lévő felépített épületeket tudjuk saját színű workereinkkel befoglalni - ez nagyon hasznos lépés tud lenni, persze ez is pénzbe kerül (és egy james bond workerbe). Ha ügyes vagy a fenti akciók mindegyikét meglépted egy körben is akár, mert bár a főtáblára körönként csak 1 workert tehetsz le, a saját felépített épületeid minden üres helyét feltöltheted. A játék fix pontszámig megy, pontokat csak az atombombák és a tesztrobbantások adnak. Összetett, több működő stratégiát lehetővé tevő rendszer, közepesen nehéz euro, tökéletesen működő tematikával.

RATING: 7
Pleasantly surprised. Interesting worker placement where you get buildings (cards) that you can also put workers on (like Le Havre), but on your turn you can place as many workers (three varieties) as you want, but only one on the main board. Temporary "contract" workers is also kinda nifty. But there are a couple things I'm not so sure how I feel about:
1) Air Strikes. It seems like you need to buy bombers as a deterrent, but in a multiplayer game it's not in anyone's best interest to actually bomb anyone else. It's the typically thing about you hurt someone, but don't get anything for it, so the guy who was completely out of it comes out best of all.
2) You don't get anything when people use your buildings. Yes, they have to pay to do so, but it goes to the bank. Seems like the player who owns it should get something.
Very nice presentation. My kids would like this.
---
Update: My son LOVES this. Perhaps his favorite boardgame.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
In The Manhattan Project the players are going to build an abstract infrastructure to gather sufficient resources and manpower to produce atomic bombs. The player who obtains a certain number of bomb points the quickest, wins the game. Right off the bat, the author flips the bird to softie subjects and turns [i]really[/i] serious WMDs into an abstracted worker placement game—a race, no less! I like how he thinks.

There are, at first sight, a few good aspects to this title which make it stand out a little from the average slew of worker placement games. For one thing there is no separate phase of taking back placed workers: it is an action you can take. That opens up a number of novel interactions. Now in fairness to other designers the idea wasn't unique at Spiel 2011: I’ve seen it used in two other titles at least. The second good idea is spying: using the buildings of other people before they can. Insidious, effective, and introducing a number of new strategies. Again, this too was present in other games, in similar or slightly different forms. Third good idea: destroying other player's buildings by means of a simple combat mechanism. (No, not with the nukes. That would be a little over the top.) The victim can repair his buildings, but it will cost time and money, and money may not be that engine’s strong point. Dastardly effective at times. So, all in all, a more fluid and interactive game than usual for this genre.

[b]Update[/b] So, a few plays under my belt, usually at the extreme ends of the supported player numbers. I'm not sure the game is meant to be played at these ends. With 5, it simply takes too long, especially when players become agressive and bomb quick players out of the game. It's a valid way to proceed, but if done too early the effect is basically semi-permanent player elimination. Also, it doesn't feel very nice to be spending all that effort just for one to two bombs. With 2, the interaction is simple and too direct: you only have the other player to contend with, so if one starts to bomb, the other starts to spy. The rating for at these numbers is decidedly lower than the ideal 7 I gave. I hope that a compromise is reached at 3 or 4. (I'm guessing 3.)

[b]Update 2[/b] The more I play this game, the less I'm beginning to like it. The strong points I alluded to above are basically [i]all[/i] you can do to hinder other players, and what they boil down to is artificially lengthening the game. The game does not reward agression on its own: it is only the fear of losing that drives a player to become agressive because there is simply no other way available to him to reel in an efficient engine. It is leader bashing in optima forma, taking three steps forward and then two steps back. Or even two and a half. And once all the engines have been reduced to a patchwork quilt matters become so dependent on chaotic-tactical timing of placement and withdrawal of workers that you can't really tell anymore if a win was deserved or not. While The Manhattan Project is probably good fare for those who like these swings of fortune until, finally, someone beats the rest by a noselength, it is not the kind of game I prefer. This is most unfortunate as I really dig the mature subject matter (yielding the game a bonus point). But it is not my game, and never will be, either.

I probably ought to move it to the trade pile.

[b]Update 3[/b] Sold off.

RATING: 2
Who knew a game about doing physics could be so boring!  It made me physic-ally ill to play this game. Ha-HA! Also, the burger I had while playing the game was suspect.

Now credit where credit is due, they managed to make a bunch of hairy, unwashed scientists look adorable.  Building a mass-murdering bomb is so cute!  But there's no fun.  None.  Put out a pile of workers.  Fun, I got some stuff.  Take them all back and that's all I can do for a turn.  Less fun!  It's like, a non-turn.  It's like a turn in Smallworld where you put your race into decline.  Strategically important?  Sure.  Means the other player(s) get to have two fun turns while you sit around doing nothing?  Also sure!

And the decisions you make just aren't thrilling.  Collect some yellowcake and some different workers, so I can move this little marker up a little, and hey, I got a little closer to making a bomb.  Hooray!  It's a fourth quarter results report playing out before my very eyes.  Thin theme, no fun, and dead turns make this the least of all worker placement games outside of an actual college counselor's office.

RATING: 9
Played 3x.
This is maybe the quintessential worker placement game for me. It's light enough to be a gateway and, I think, interesting enough to play over and over again. 

There is a LOT of player interaction for a worker placement game. Not only are you competing for spots, you're also competing for buildings because they can give you unblockable spots in the future. Your strategy has to change based on what buildings are available during your turn and you can be really mean by buying a building that someone else desperately needs.

Plus there are the bombing and espionage spots. I haven't explored the bombing strategy much but the espionage strategy, when executed at key moments, can really turn a game around. Timing is everything in this game--when to buy a building, when to take back your workers, when to run your engine and build a bomb. Despite all of this, the game tends to be quick. Maybe too quick--but it's easy enough to extend the VP score that will end the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
The Manhattan Project has a lot of positives.  The worker placement and engine building is well themed.  It also allows for friendly or confrontational play.  The learning curve is pretty easy, and it only takes a few turns to start building momentum.  We didn't use the spy or combat much in our first play, but there was still plenty to keep us busy.  The replay value looks strong, and I would recommend at least one play.  The only two downsides for me were the lack of hidden scoring & a little downtime between turns.  Even with these minor complaints it still scores high for me.

RATING: 6
Interesting variation on the worker placement mechanic. You actually get to [i]attack[/i] people. And I don't mean in some abstract eurogamey way, either. You build planes and bombers, then go blow the crap out of other nations if you can. The nation cards are a nice touch, too. I suspect a bit of influence from [i]High Frontier[/i].

The good news (for eurogamers anyway) is that the military aspect is largely meant as a way to foil game leaders. By itself it's not a path to victory and if you focus too heavily on the military you're almost certainly going to lose. You're probably better off building a spy and just stealing their buildings. Another problem is that it's not always easy to tell just who the leader is.

While I really enjoyed the ability to directly affect your opponents through attacks and spying, I think the chaos of the buildings (both in terms of arrival timing and ability) can be a downer. I think it's crucial to get at least one University and if you can't get one, and you can't get a spy, you're in deep trouble. The game does sort of abrubtly end, too.

On a side note, this is definitely more interesting than [i]Lords of Waterdeep[/i] which many people are comparing this game to at the time I write this comment. There is no comparison. They are nothing alike, and this game is far more interesting and innovative in my opinion.

RATING: 7
[2014-04-04 Rating: 8.5 -> 7]
After some more plays of the Manhattan Project, I feel like I have to lower my rating to a 7. Don't get me wrong, I still think this is a fine and very clever game but it is just too mean for me. I do hugely enjoy building my engine and racing to the finish line. What I don't like is the possibility to throw a stick into the spokes of someone else riding at full speed. That's just not my idea of fun.

[2013-02-04 Rating: n.a. -> 8.5]

Note that I've only played the game twice. So I might adjust might rating after some more plays since there are some things within this game that might kill the fun for me. But first, let's get to what I like about [i]The Manhattan Project (TMP)[/i]:

Basically, this is another worker placement/resource conversion/engine building game. But, true to it's theme, it has a nice race feeling to it and I do really like the aspect of the different workers. Preferable, you would like to use the specialists only for the tasks in which they give you the "biggest bang for the buck". But sometimes it's better to simply use them in a "suboptimal" to block someone else from using a specific action. Another thing I like about the workers is the fact that you won't get them back at the end of your turn automatically. Instead, you have a skip a whole turn to retrieve them from where you used them. This crucial aspect of the game reminds me a little bit of the card management aspect you have in games like "Illiad". It introduces a great timing component into the game. So, a big thumbs up for the worker management part of the game.

Yet another thing I found interesting is the fact, that the buildings enter the game randomly. So it is possible, that a very strong building is placed in the starting setup and, based on your seating position, there's nothing you can do to hinder your opponents from grabbing it. This is a little bit offset by the fact that you can directly attack your opponents buildings with Airstrikes or the use of the Espionage action which let's you use their buildings (think Ora et Labora). 

These two mechanics (Airstrike and Espionage) allow for a good dose of (negative) direct player interaction. As always, I'm normally not a great fan of this mechanics. But I haven't made up my mind about them in TMP. While I had one game where I played quite good and was in the lead when my buildings got bombed, I had the feeling that this wasn't really the end of the world since I had still access to the Espionage action and made good use of it. While I couldn't fully regain speed and the game was won by someone who didn't get bombed, I have the feeling that you might still overcome the results of a bombing raid as long as you can still use the Espionage action or have enough money to repair the damage. But I will have to see if this is true in future games.

All in all, I did really enjoy my two plays of TMP and I'm looking forward to my next game. So, give this game a shot if you're more on the euro side of games.

RATING: 9.25
This is one of my favorite worker placement games.  It has some fresh new ideas (or at least a new take on some tried and true ideas) and is integrated excellently. It is one of the most thematic Euro games available.

The last few rounds can sometimes degenerate into long AP turns due to the amount of options, and especially when a player might be able to end the game but they just can't figure out how.  It also has a conflict element which some people might not appreciate (though I think it is implemented perfectly).  Overall, this is a very solid game.

RATING: 4
Players are major powers, trying to develop the first atomic bombs.  By the numbers worker placement game... with a few wrinkles.  Workers come in three flavours, worker, engineer and scientist, which restrict where they can be placed.  You build buildings, which get the economy of cash, yellow cake, and more workers up and running.  Buildings earn more resources, and turn cake into uranium and plutonium, which go into the bombs...  the victory points.  Nice theming for a game, although lightly applied here - it really could be anything.  Fun to explore a few times, but this one is a bit long, and quite nasty.  Played three times now, and this one has a short half life.  Get the engine up, mince your sausage machine, and hope you win.  Go and play Tribune: Primus Inter Pares, and see what a master game designer can do with worker placement.

RATING: 5
A very solid entry into the worker placement genre.  The art goes a long way - it looks like a cross between Fallout and Legos.   The main twist (I hesitate to call it an innovation) is that there is no "return home" phase for your workers.  You have to forfeit a turn to get all your workers back, so some turns you may not wish to place all of them and save them for the next round.   The downside of all this is that the board remains very crowded (with 5 players, anyway) and you have to know when other players are going to "return home" and open things up for you. The theme is really strongly implemented and that helps the game succeed where otherwise it might just be a jumble of borrowed mechanisms.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is one of the best worker placement games I have ever played and to be honest, I am not a fan of them in general.  However, what's really interesting about TMP is that the mechanics and the theme integrate so well with each other.  Unlike your run-of-the-mill point-salady placement game, most of the game will be about engine-building (nobody will have points on the board for quite some time), and each worker available to you has a specific role.  While this has generally been handled in newer games using dice, TMP's workers aren't so abstract - you have laborers, engineers, and scientists, and they each contribute to thematically-sound things; your laborers do mundane, but necessary work; your engineers build and design; your scientists churn out chemicals and compounds.  The key to this one is managing exactly when to take all your workers back as they don't come back to you of their own volition; you either put one down or you take -all- of them back, freeing up the spaces they occupied for some other dastardly player to snatch.

Moreover, there is quite a bit of interaction between players in the form of espionage and bombing runs, which is rare in a typical WP game.  While it can feel a little unfair to get your stuff bombed to smithereens, the game still remains tight in the points race and there are always ways to use the main board to get what you want.

The only concern that I have is that it can drag on, especially if the aforementioned player interaction is exploited.  A game of peace could end quickly, but it's much more likely that there will be some scrapping, and for each bout amongst players, tack on another 20 minutes to the game, give or take.

RATING: 6
The Manhattan Project is a decent worker placement game. Each player owns 12 workers in 3 types but at the start of the game players only have access to 4 workers of the basic type (unless you are playing a high player count and are low in the player order). The resources are: money (used for various things), yellowcake (used for money, plutonium, and uranium), uranium (used for VP and sometimes plutonium), plutonium (used for VP), Bombers (damage buildings and can net VP), and Fighters (destroys other fighters/bombers and protects your buildings from being damaged). I guess you could consider workers as another type of resource since your basic worker is limited in what he can do and the other two types have different functions and need to be acquired. On your turn you have the option of placing workers or retrieving them and understanding this ebb and flow seems to be quite important. The game ends when one player completes an action that pushes him to or above a set number of VP based on players which is quite different than a lot of other games.

I don't like the end game. Why? In one turn a player can play a bomb card from his hand and load it in the same turn and get almost 4/5's of the VP needed to win the game... I'm thinking of the Pluonium Bomb that's worth 34 points after an implosion test and can then be loaded on the same turn for an additional 5 points. In a 4-player game you only need 50 VP to win the game. It does well in ramping up the tension a bit because the game can end quite suddenly. This could be a good or bad thing depending on your preferences. 

Even though people will mostly start with the same number of workers, I'm reminded of Agricola in that there will be an asymmetric number of workers as the game is unfolds. At this point, I don't know if or how much of a snowball effect this would have but I'm reminded of Agricola. In Agricola, I felt this worker issue bridged a threshold to the point where I thought it was a balance issue and made the game less enjoyable. I'm not saying it is an issue in TMP but it's definitely something I would be paying attention to in further games.

I will note this game has more direct interaction than in many other worker placement games which, again, could be a good or bad thing based on your preferences. Our game never saw many uses Airstrikes and I think this had to do with the way the buildings came out. We never saw many factories and the good factories didn't show up until near the of the end game. I think this is actually a good thing because the way the buildings come out can have a dramatic effect on how the game is played, adding replay value to TMP.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A worker placement game that's predicated on tempo and not leaving yourself vulnerable to attack. In a counter-intuitive stroke, the game is more about letting other payers build great buildings – and using them so they can’t – and building only so-so ones yourself (so they’re left unused). 

The uniqueness on the w-p stuff here is that you either place dudes or recall dudes, and managing that tempo relative to your opponents is quite fun. The espionage tech allows you to use other players' buildings but only one player can use the tech track at a time so you want to be placing soon as another player recalls his piece from it.

If you want an interactive worker placement game, here it is, because you can build up fighters and bombers to attack other players and destroy their stuff. And I could probably do without that element as I generally like my euros to be indirectly confrontational. We did have a case of a player getting attacked by multiple players and feeling pretty sour about things. Consequently, it never got traction with my group and I moved it on.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.125
I love, love, love:
- the various worker types and their bonuses in different spots
- the rather cute theme
- the cardboard worker components
- the uranium and plutonium tracks (so resource clutter is minimized)
- the place-on-the-main-board-then-your-own mechanism
- that recalling workers is its own action--no defined rounds when gameplay just has to stop until everyone has passed
- that you can use another player's buildings!
- the air strikes to slow down a leader
- the solid engine-building

I dislike:
- that air strikes can almost drag the game to a crawl
- that some of the worker spots don't make sense thematically
- not knowing how the Bribe pile makes thematic sense??
- the rather abrupt style of game end

Final verdict: A game that has a lot of mechanisms that I really enjoy with worker placement and have fortunately spilled over into other great games. Theme is cute, components are just right, and engine-building is just perfection.

RATING: 6
First off, the graphic design of The Manhattan Project is simply gorgeous. The thematic connection to the real Manhattan Project seems a little weak, though.

The gameplay itself cannot quite keep up with the graphical representation. It's a solid worker placement game with a couple of twists and a few caveats. The most obvious, of course, is the direct confrontation and possible leader bashing through air strikes which is something that is sure to polarise. A more serious detractor, however, seems to be that there is very little strategic variance: Since the only way to score points is by building bombs and there are only two different bomb types, manoeuverability is often fairly low and with that in mind, the game runs a bit longer than it should.

RATING: 5.25
Game looks great, and well produced.  Did not really enjoy my first play, but will likely give it another go at some point.

The game is fairly tactical, chaotic, and brief.  Card draws make a significant difference, and players can interact easily to king-make.  The pick on the leader aspect is not well-tuned for a strategy title IMO, at least based on the first play.  When commenting on this, however, the other players echoed the lack of control.  Some examples: building plutonium became virtually worthless when an all uranium bomb draw came out, and was tied up for a long time by a player blocking the bomb design action to target a specific player.  If anyone gets to a lead, it's very easy to target them.  It does not appear to break the game, as there are enough work-arounds.  It does however, make you wonder why bother working your engine up when there's no point if others are behind you.  Building protection is of little use as air combat is one for one, and there's no amount of protection that is going to help you with 4 players targeting you.  I'm ok with catch-up mechansisms, as long as they are more opaque, leading to calculated sacrifice.  In this game, it's really lacking, as air power is so easily built, it's just a matter of pointing your finger at the leader and getting a quick agreement.

That said, there are plenty of players who like Munchkin like chaos, and lots of take-that leading to a surprise outcome.

May revisit these comments if I give it another try.

RATING: 7.8
A lighter worker placement game.  Everyone works on their own engine to build a certain number of points of bombs.  The game can end very quickly once players have built a number of buildings and have started their "engine" running.  It does add interactiveness via an espionage and air strike action, both of which can be annoying if you're the target:).  It also adds a twist in that recalling your workers takes a turn, so it adds consideration of timing and just how you want to space out using your workers - do you use them all and then recall them the following turn?  Or do you keep a few in reserve and hope that the player across the table with the valuable university (or other building) recalls his workers so you can sneak into his building and use it for yourself?  Or do you spread your workers out and try to block actions on the board?

RATING: 8
Just picked this up in early 2016. I love it. It's worker placement but not as "get in each others' way" as Agricola/Brew Crafters, has a clearer path to victory that is easy to understand, and the rules and game flow COMPLETELY reflect the theme of the arms race. "YOU have a huge air force? *I* had better have a huge air force! Strictly for protection, you understand." "I think that guy has designed a very powerful bomb... *I* better design a very powerful bomb, and build it before he does!" Basically, the scarier you are, and the more likely you seem to be willing to push The Button, the more victory points you get. It's good. Morbid, but good.

RATING: 8
Components: ok components
Complex: easy to teach, many things going on then
Weight: medium
Players: 4
Length: around 90 mins
Mechanics: workers placement
Decisions: a lot, where to place my worker, should I recover them or go for another one, build, bomb...
Opinion: I loved the game, with quite easy rules you have a game with lot of interaction and some kind of cold war with the bombers.
You are fighting with your rivals to build the best bombs (ok, the theme is quite, hmm, inappropriate is not the word but could be), so you have your man force and have to use them to... build buildings, improve your air force, get uranium, do some espionage, and.. build bombs!!! yea, in the end you will get point for building bombs
Your workers can be improved to scientists or engineers, and you can block other player buildings with some espionage, and... your can bomb their buildings if they don't have enough force to defend...
The interaction is what I have liked most, yes, it could have some kingmaking, so be careful with who you play it, but the way you can be mean is delicious (bombing or blocking). And this is the only flaw I could say at this moment, be aware with who you play, otherwise you have a great game here

RATING: 9
I am in love with this game, It has great depth in streamlined package . There is a lot about this game that separates it from the other worker placement games. It is a race to a set amount of points with opportunity for players to really together and slow down the leader with out the game dragging at the same time. Though it is a worker placement game it brakes a lot of the standard WP rules, and is more about building a sand box area on your player board. Once you have built a good player board the main board is just second to the game and is no longer the focus for you. This is a aspect that i love about the game and for anyone who loves engine building games even if they don't care for worker placement i would recommend this game.

RATING: 7.9

The person who taught us the game taught us the Fighter/Bomber track incorrectly and therefore nobody really used it much throughout the game. The game overstayed it's welcome a bit and took a while before you really started to build your engine, otherwise I would have liked it more. Still, I'm definitely willing to play it again as I had a really good time with it. The mechanic that allows you to keep workers on the board to block your opponents is really interesting to me.

RATING: 6
Played it twice, still not sure what to make of this game. The idea and theme are solid, the components feel good and the gameplay is well thought out for the most part.

I have two main concerns. First concern is the fighter/bomber planes aspect. It really feels bad if you don't build them and somebody then comes along and damages one of your buildings. It's immediately rendered useless but paying 5 + a worker is often too much of an investment to repair 1 point of damage. In a 4 player game, if you can get away without building planes, it gives you a big edge if your opponents are fighting and damaging each other. So you kinda want to take a risk and just focus on your engine instead. Often what happened was that 1 person went for an early attack before being forced to focus on his own engine. This left 1 player crippled while the 2 remaining players profited. 

My second concern is regarding the bomb-design drafting. It feels a bit weird that 1 person makes the investment and everyone else benefits from this. Especially because the benefits can depend greatly your position at the table. The second person has a big edge in a 4 player game compared to the last person to choose. Sometimes you're also in the position where you need a new bomb-design but going for that action will lead to a win for your opponent. So you just kind of have to fiddle around a bit? Wait until you can immediately finish the game when you grab a new design? 

Both issues might solve themselves by getting better at the game. But at the moment I'm neither hot nor cold about The Manhattan Project.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Manhattan Project is a racing efficiency euro as players vye to build the largest atomic bombs. Along with the theme, Manhattan Project is unique is its worker usage. On a turn players either place and return their workers. When choosing return, all players lose any temporary extra workers from certain parts of the board. This gives players some control of opening up and closing spaces for use. Additionally, the efficiency race is not only in gaining materials to fill contracts (bombs), but also building an engine to get the most workers in the best way. This is a rewarding race with a novel theme and some interesting twists on worker placement.

RATING: 8.5
Just when you think that worker placement is passé, there comes a game which takes this seemingly overused mechanic and modifies it so cleverly that it feels all fresh & new. 

The Manhattan Project adds: asynchronous worker retrieval, private worker spaces and the ability to occupy other players space by using the "espionage" action.  This mixes to a very dynamic gameplay where the timing of your actions becomes crucial.  A second thing I really like about this game is the reaaally slow start which, after about 70-90 minutes suddenly and dramatically accelates when evybody rushes to finish his bombs. By now I've seen several games where the victory of one player was literally hanging by a thread (i.e. only half a turn)

Finally, I like the story of the game. Granted: building atomic bombs is not what you call the most family friendly theme but personally I'm just sick of all those pasted-on medivial merchants/priests/kings/whatever themes which we find in far too many Eurogames.  TMP tells a story and it tells this story well.  Getting the materials, designing the bombs, commanding air strikes to cripple enemies plants and enrichment centers: this all just fits just perfectly together.

From my current gaming experience I'd give this game a solid 9. However, I'm not sure how well it is balanced.  Uranium bombs *seem* to be a little overpower in contrast to their Plutonium counterparts.  It'll take a few more plays to deeper analyze this.  But all in all, this a really great game which was very well received by my different gaming groups.

Edit:
After 6 plays I stick to a rating of 8 (with a tendency towards 9).  I've played the game with 2, 3 and 4 players and would strongly recommend it with 3 and 4.  2 player works as well but is a little bit more predictable and with less interesting dynamics.

RATING: 4
Below-average worker placement game where you build bombs for victory points, with some good ideas but poor development. Your workers come in three types: laborers, scientists and engineers, with some actions requiring or benefiting from specific ones. The game is unique in that you may place them all at once if you have enough buildings (just one worker in the central board), and you must spend a turn to get them back.

This causes the early game to be a building race. Once your engine is running, the only thing that may stop it is an opponent using your buildings (and thus preventing you to use them yourself) via the espionage action. It didn't happen much in my two games, but I can see how this may rapidly become very annoying. The blocking may go on indefinitely, especially with neutral workers, which are only removed when the owner of the building chooses to retrieve his own workers. Then the same opponent or another may have an opportunity to occupy those buildings again before the owner takes another turn, and so on. Bombing a building is also possible, but I haven't seen it happen, because it harms both players involved. And it would just slow the game down.

If all goes well, the players may get all their workers from the general supply too quickly and even take some one-use neutral workers every turn. Depending on the buildings a player has, he can deplete almost the entire supply of neutral workers in one turn, leaving nothing to the other players. This, along with the blocking of buildings, makes the timing of worker retrieval perhaps too important.

However, what sometimes (apparently too often) breaks the game is the completely random order in which the buildings appear - a problem that would have been easily solved by a competent designer by simply splitting the deck into three or four stacks. My second game had a strain of mines in the beginning, leading to a surplus of yellow cakes for everyone and no efficient way to convert them into uranium or plutonium.

Yet another negative point is that the game ends abruptly. It's a race to 50 points, but as most bombs are worth a lot of points, the victory condition might as well be to make three bombs first.

The board is ugly and the art style does not appeal to me. The production quality is good, but wooden or plastic pieces would have worked better than the cardboard tokens for workers. I find the theme dull, but it works reasonably well and is somewhat novel, even though it serves the old and tired efficiency exercise of resource conversion.

RATING: 6
The Manhattan project is a light worker placement game similar in game weight to Stone Age. I like how adding cards to my tableau creates new places my workers can work as well as the cards I choose are building a resource production engine. The espionage action is neat how it allows a player to work on an opponent's building which clogs the building so they can't use it. I only played it once, mind you. Nobody bombed anyone else. I imagined that if I chose to bomb someone else, it would hurt not only the bombee, but that it would slow me down too because I did not choose a productive work area on the main board--so that maybe the other players not involved would be the greatest beneficiaries of a bombing action. Overall, I didn't find the game compelling. I do appreciate how the theme is original and executed well in the game, but it isn't a theme I like. I will play it again, but I am not in a rush to do so and I don't feel the need to get a copy of the Manhattan Project.

RATING: 9
This is an intense, beautiful, well-balanced, and fun game. On your first play you may feel that Espionage or Air Strikes are over or under-powered, but I urge you to get a few games in and realize how exceptionally well-designed these systems are.

This is not a multi-player solitaire euro. You will be directly interfering with the other players. If you are behind, you must collude with other players to pull back the leader. It is a race to the finish, and there is no second place! Have fun!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
On the surface it's a great worker placer, but there are a few problems with it.  It has a great theme and lovely artwork, particularly on the building and bomb cards (the bombs even have their own names), the workers are also very 'pickup-able'.  The idea of the game is to create raw radioactive material to convert into Plutonium/Uranium with which to build bombs.  The supply line is fairly long and complex and offers lots of ways to get to your goal, some people will concentrate on cashflow, whereas some may concentrate on raw materials and it isn't clear to me after a few plays what is the best strategy, so that's good.

My main problem with this one is the attacking mechanic - the players can invest money and workers in creating bombers and fighter planes to attack the other players, but it's a very arbitrary system with little actual mechanic to it - if you have more bombers, you get to blow up the other players' stuff - which is poor and sort of ruins the flow and rhythm of the game.  Still, it's a nice game and well worth a play, maybe try to ignore the attacking mechanics to see if it improves things.

RATING: 6
The controversial leader-bashing air stike mechanics is not a problem here.  _Formalized_ pacts are included in the rules not without a reason...  And you can prepare yourself against air strikes.

The real problem is that the game is very bland.  Standard worker placement mechanics works ok, but you always have so many possible good options that you don't feel tension or 'tightness' of the design.  On the contrary - every player shuffles - almost on mass scale - his workers and resources back and forth, always busy with exercising many options at hand.  To the point of the game becoming a race.  And I clearly dislike racing element in economic/optimization games.

With 5 players downtime is annoying.  If you want to try the game, give it a shot with max. 4 players.

The theme is strong mostly on paper.  You don't feel it so well during the play.

RATING: 6
Pretty classic worker placement game, but with the twist of being able to screw with other players pretty heavily. The bombing aspect didn't seem so bad compared to the spying. Things can just work out in such a way that you basically never have access to certain very important things. And the only way to stop this is to play less efficiently and slow yourself down, which will absolutely lose you the game, since it's a race. And it's a weird race, the first person to hit the required number of victory points wins, there's no chance of equal turns. This is especially a problem since being the first player got you an advantage in options at the beginning and gives you a turn advantage in ending the game. The small amount of extra money you start with does little to make up for this. Overall just a game where things can come together in a way to make it seem like you can't do anything, which to me was no fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The Manhattan Project is my favorite worker placement game.

I love the engine building in this one: all players are trying to do the same thing, but the game provides multiple paths do so. And unlike many worker placement games, there's always something to do - even if it is just pulling workers to get ready for a monster turn next time up.

It's also an underrated two-player game as the timing of worker placements and pulls become extremely important.

The only criticism is that the attacking mechanism (Air Strikes) can be abused by players who want to see a certain player lose. I recommend not playing this game with players like that.

I see this game being a long-time if not permanent part of my collection.

2013-03-18

Initial Rating: 1.5 (March 2013)

What a disappointment, I had huge hopes for this one but the game is total crap as it is. It feels underdeveloped and the way it handles the worker placement mechanic is clearly broken. The first player has an unbelievable advantage and will always win the game as he can play all his workers without giving the other players time to react. The last player never had any interesting things to do and never had the chance to spy. 

The theme is interesting (and was what called me to the game) but it feels pasted on, the game components are very sturdy and nice to touch (and that’s why it receive a 1.5 rating). I feel there can be a good game in here but for now it is a compilation of good ideas.  

I cannot believe the game is, as of today, ranked here in BGG at the 269 spot, which leads me to think that perhaps the player that read and explained the rules made a mistake somewhere. I will read the rules today and see for myself. I really hope that there is a BIG rules mistake or else I did a BIG mistake buying this game. As it is, The Manhattan Project is the worst worker placement game out there.

Current Rating: 1.5

Current Rating: 6.0
[hr]
2013-04-05

At last I got to play this with 4 players and the correct set of rules. The verdict? I like the game :D. As I love worker placement games, it is natural that I like The Manhattan Project. I really like that there are no rounds in the game, instead a player can play one worker in the central board and all he wants in his buildings or retire all his workers. This adds another level of complexity to consider and I really dig it. It that sense this game is (somewhat) similar to Tzolk'in: The Mayan Calendar but (for me) this game is deeper and superior so I like it more.  Also The Manhattan Project offers 3 different kinds of workers, this is the first time I have seen this in a worker placement game. :thumbsup:

I´m a little worried about the attacks, they are necessary but it seems that if you attack, you let yourself open to an attack. What I’m saying is that attacking cost too much. Also, usually, the attack mechanism serves to stop the current leader and (nowadays) I don’t particularly enjoy the “bash the leader” situation. I also feel the game ends too abruptly.

In conclusion, a good worker placement game, not my favorite (Agricola, Caylus, Dungeon Lords and Pret a Porter are superior) but still good. I would play it from time to time and I plan to keep my copy.

Current Rating: 6.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Light-hearted game of atom bomb building in the 1940s. Kind of a hybrid euro/ameritrash; I've played other such hybrids, like Fire & Axe and Bootleggers, but this is really the first hybrid worker-placement game I've tried. It's got the euro worker-placement strategy influences but with a strong dose of theme-mechanism connections (bombers that can destroy buildings, nations with appropriate special powers, espionage, etc) and of course (appropriate given the theme) much more confrontational than most euros.

What stuck out for me is the unusual game rhythm (no rounds, rather, you keep putting workers out until you're out and have to recall all of them) and different categories of workers (laborers, engineers, scientists).

Art design is awesome in a 1940s retro/propoganda style, reminds me of the art from Fallout minus the sci-fi parts. Overall production is very high quality and sturdy, the worker chits are seriously practically bricks, I've never seen such thick chits. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
For some reason I wasn't supper excited about the theme. On my first game, the initial motivation and choices to make were not very clear. I felt unsure what I should do.

Also, the hidden scoring and suddenness of game end left a bad taste in my mouth. I had no idea how close we were to the end and no idea who was even really in the running. 

The take-that element was interested and I can see why you would want to hide scoring to prevent king bashing, but without knowing who was leading, I couldn't do much other than to maximize my own goals.

In our 5 player game, there wasn't many scoring opportunities. The end was sudden and unexpected. There was no alert to who was going to possibly end the game and no way to prepare or switch strategies.

RATING: 4
Always start feedback with the positive: Manhattan Project features great artwork. Ok, so much for that. 
Too bad, that the gameplay is only feasible for Ameritrash lovers. Manhattan Project is one of the worst cases of crossover games that nullify 80% of euro-mechanism based gameplay by adding one core element of direct player targeting that severely punishes successful players. Better don't stand out, don't build anything too obviously efficient. Otherwise, you are guaranteed to be the first loser as you have a large target cross on your forehead. Alien Frontiers suffered from the same design decision.
The single remarkable element of the Euro core is the decision when to take back workers although most of the time players just do it when they have none left. This mechanism was implemented in a far superior way in Tzolkin a year later.

RATING: 10
Being an avid worker placement game player (just look at my list of games) I have to say that Manhattan Project delivered on all fronts.  The game plays so smoothly, and after about 3ish plays, I think I had the game down pat, but it still comes to surprise me how far I felt I was mid game from winning only to turn out we're neck and neck by the end.  Even with AP prone players, the game felt fast.  I think partially that is due to the fact that the game encourages you to build "machines" to further your agenda.  So AP players still have a ton of choices, but the good ones are generally laid out for them and unless someone is contesting the same exact strategy, there is little conflict and they can finish their turns fast enough to not bother the rest of the group.  The game also allows a bit of plan B where if your main strat fails, you can still rely on some clever moves to either slow your opponents down, or catch up with some lucky card draws, or just out right get lucky and pick the route less traveled and win the game through no contest.  The vast array of ways to win in the game makes it a very fun game to try your luck / wit.  The next game usually boils down to, "will they play the same way I'm thinking?  Or maybe I'll try something new and hope the bomb cards flip as I expect them?  Or will the other players fight so much I can sneak in this not so popular strategy and actually still win?"  That kind of meta gaming is what keeps me coming back.  I play only with the expansion, and I highly recommend everyone have this game in their collection along with the expansion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Eh.  This is okay but other worker placement type games do it better and are more enjoyable.

The game play is good and there are a lot of options for how to use your workers.  Also, it's possible to have quite a few workers available for selecting among the available options which allows for some good choices.  The feature whereby players choose either to place workers or remove workers during a game turn is nice and allows some flexibility in how one develops their game plan.  This also allows for interesting opportunities as players can try to align the flow of their game to alternate with that of other players (i.e. when he/she is removing workers, I'm ready to start placing workers or, just as their ready to start placing workers, I go in and block the prime options).

The espionage option allows a player to use other players buildings thus locking that player out from his own buildings.  Being locked out of your own buildings can really limit your options and depending on how long your locked out (not always your decision) it can certainly have an impact on your game plan.  Only one player at a time can occupy and and use the espionage space.  It's very likely that this could be dominated by one or two players and if you happen to be out of sync with when this spot is available then you can really be screwed and this game won't be any fun.

The scoring seems odd.  Rather than a smooth, steady climb up a point scale, players earn points in large chunks at a time.  For example (and I believe this to be accurate), points are earned by building bombs which can net a player anywhere from 10 to 30 points at a time.  Note that the first player to accumulate 45 points wins the game.

It seems that the artwork is a big draw for the game and it IS very nice; quite good in fact.  However the style doesn't "wow" me, especially compared to how others have marveled over it.

The theme doesn't really work for me.  This has nothing to do with morality or whether or not it's offensive (believe me), it's just odd.  Yes, it's definitely different but that's not a driving factor for me.

The quality of the components is good.  The quality of the individual worker tiles, being of very thick cardboard, is very impressive. 

Writing these comments has made me think more about the game.  Perhaps more experience with the game would increase my rating but I'm not going to actively seek out that experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Has some good ideas, but really feels like it needed some more development. The place/take back workers mechanism could've been interesting, but seems to just come down to gaming when to let someone else into espionage. I suspect it could be a lot more interesting if the espionage mechanic was more involved than a single space on the board, which would also solve the problem that it becomes a case of taking the same few moves to run your engine later in the game (it reminds me of Cuba a bit in this regard).

There are other more minor issues with the buildings - some seem to exist only to fill up the building track - and the planes; the few times I've played that aspect of the game has just come down to who gets the biggest fighter factory.

So some nice ideas, but pretty flawed execution, and this is definitely one I tend to avoid.

RATING: N/A
The Manhattan Project is a low-luck, mostly open information efficiency game in which players compete to build and operate the most effective atomic bomb program. Players do not "nuke" each other, but conventional air strikes are allowed against facilities.

The game features worker placement with a twist: there are no rounds and no end-of-round administration. Players retrieve their workers when they choose to or are forced to (by running out).

An espionage action allows a player to activate and block an opponent's building, representing technology theft and sabotage.

RATING: 4
BOMBE!
In diesem Arbeiter-Einsetz-Spiel haben die Spieler 3 verschieden artige Arbeiter (die erst im laufe des Spiels verfügbar werden). Mit Ingenieuren kann man billig Häuser bauen, mit Wissenschaftlern Uran und Plutonium herstellen. Um schlussendlich eine Bombe zu bauen, die Siegpunkte bringt brauche ich sowohl Ingenieure, als auch Wissenschaftler.
Im Spiel muss man durch die Arbeiterfelder Yellow-Cake und Geld sammeln. Aus diesen beiden Ressourcen macht man mit einem anderen Feld dann Uran oder Plutonium und daraus dann mit weiteren Aktionsfeldern Bomben, die die Siegpunkte darstellen (je mehr Ressourcen die kosten, desto mehr SP!).
Durch Bau von Gebäuden kann man die Ressourcenausbeute weiter erhöhen, vor allem, da das einsetzen von Arbeitern auf eigenen Gebäuden keine Aktion kostet. So konnte ich gegen Ende des Spiels innerhalb eines Zuges einen Arbeiter aufs Brett stellen und alle anderen 11 auf eigene Gebäude und das war toll!
Leider finde ich das Spiel nicht so toll. Zum einen gibt es hier und da thematische Lücken. Zum anderen zieht sich der Start sehr, da man ohne Gebäude auf die schwachen Aktionen auf dem Brett angewiesen ist. Gefühlt muss man erst seine Arbeiter in die Universität schicken um an Ingenieure und Wissenschaftler zu kommen, dann baut man massenweise Gebäude und Flugzeuge (man kann damit Gegner angreifen oder Angriffe abwehren) um dann die Ressourcenmaschiene zu starten und Bomben (Siegpunkte) zu bauen.
Nein, leider durchgefallen!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I wish the worker placement on the main board for this game was a bit more claustrophobic. Most of the time even if someone goes to a spot I wanted to go to there is a second spot that is only marginally worse for me (one exception being the espionage space).

While I initially thought the espionage and air strikes spaces felt superfluous, I think they really help to bring down the leader down to size (you may have to go all out if you plan to make bombing effective though, as otherwise you'll waste too many actions performing air strikes), although since you only need to make about 3 bombs to win the game that can sometimes be hard to figure out. Also, espionage makes timing when you pull off your workers actually matter rather than being able to alternate between using all your buildings in a round followed by pulling everyone back.

Therefore, I think this game while having a very Euro engine works best with players that like high interaction, as otherwise the alternation of putting all your workers on the board followed by pulling everyone off next turn makes this game feel pretty formulaic.

Overall, I think the design of this 90-minuteish game could have been a lot cleaner (there are a lot of steps you have to perform before your actions materialize as points, and some spots on the board feel almost unnecessary), but the unique theme and a good amount of interaction without being overboard (barring someone dedicating all their time to increasing fighters/bombers and focus firing someone out of spite) makes this a pretty good game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Probably my favorite worker placement game. I like the mix of personal-board engine building and main-board key action fights. The theme is exceptionally well integrated, and good graphic design (particularly on the main board) helps explain the flow of the game from mining raw materials to assembling bombs for VPs. "Multiple paths to victory" is something of a stretch, as VPs only come via bombs, but there are lots of ways to tailor your engine to pursue that goal in various ways.

There are some direct-conflict mechanisms (espionage, air raids) that may be off-putting to pure-Euro worker placement fans.

I think there's one significant shortcoming in the graphic design, in that player board "any worker is acceptable" is indicated by a "?" worker face while the main board indicates the same by showing no face. The main board should use the same iconography as the player boards. Once you know the game, it's a non-issue, but it's been a hurdle nearly every time I've taught the game.

RATING: 10
For me, this game is better than Agricola.  The blocking mechanism is better, yet the mechanics are intuitive and the games are tight.  Some might complain that if you've played this a lot, a new player can still give you a run for your money and not like that.  Well, I'd disagree with that.  As an experienced player, you have definite advantages over a new player.  But they can still pick it up and do well.  And to me, that speaks of a well-made game.  You don't need to be a veteran gamer to enjoy this, but if you are, there is plenty of depth and difficult decision-making to be had.  Best of all, it's all about playing the other people less than the system itself.

RATING: 5
The first play was fun, but after 3 plays I'm unlikely to play again, the reasons being:

1) There aren't many paths you can take; I won my first game by doing the 'obvious' things in order, and couldn't find a significantly different path to victory in my next two plays.

2) I like the idea of interaction via espionage and air strikes, and these played a big role in my 3rd game (which I also won). But really these bogged down the game and slowed it so much (e.g. my opponent and I would espionage on each other escalating to level 6 and blocking the other's options since they only had one move on main board and few left on their own, and likewise with bombing and causing damage to money supply, slowing down repairs). So by the end we just wanted it over even though it was a very close game (I won, but opponent could win on their next turn).

Still, I did have fun on the first game, so I'd say worth trying out.

RATING: 8
Cool game, and very different feeling considering the amount of worker placement games I play. I definitely like it. The directions are great, if somewhat repetitive. The components are nice, the gameplay is relatively simple and the theme is the best part. I've only played with 2, and I think it worked out pretty well with that number. Although the interaction would be much better with more players, I'd be concerned about the downtime. Even with 2, there's a pretty good deal of downtime between many turns, especially if you have players prone to AP. Speaking of interaction, the game felt a lot like multiplayer solitaire at times, which isn't bad, but sort of unexpected considering the theme. I can see how they've tried to implement interaction (air strikes, sharing resources, negotiation, espionage, etc.), but those things aren't happening every turn. Don't get me wrong--it's not a Feld, but it's not Ameritrashy either, if that's your bag. Since I'm a eurogamer, I'm not particularly bothered by the lack of interaction, but it's something that should be pointed out, since I think the interaction level is somewhat oversold. It might not sound like it, but I really like this game. My wife likes it more than me, but I'd be happy to get it back to the table sometime soon. 

*Played with Nations promo and Second Stage expansion
