RATING: 8
Good theme, I saw the theme ahead of the mechanics.
The game plays well except in two cases:
You get a bad shuffle on the resource cards which can starve everyone of resources.  The deck probably needs an A deck / B deck approach.  It has mini A deck of starter cards.  
Like power grid, you can end up with a lot of cards no one benefits from buying so they just sit there.
However, minor starving can be compensated for and there are different strategies for winning.  We had 3 approaches going in our game and almost all of us were one card from winning at the end.
I wanted to play it more but there was only one copy at BGG with the publisher.
I didn't like the nation cards as much.  I also thought the spy action caused the game to come to a stop for a few minutes every time it came available.
The game has a very clever mechanism for simultaneous play most the time but it freezes up while someone is picking who to use their spies on.
I enjoyed it and will play it again.  It was one of three games at BGG which felt "new" and not a mashup of elements of other games.

RATING: 6
03/27/12 Played a three-player game in 91 minutes. 
The Manhattan Project is noteworthy for its unique theme and worker placement twist (the "call back"). Unfortunately the theme doesn't really interest me. Pity, for its *very* well thought out. As to the twist of call back. I can't fathom what real world item the publisher is trying to mimic. Why would any country send people to school, do research, build aircraft, mine ore, etc. then one day say, hey guys, why don't you all go on vacation until we need you again. Huh?!  Now maybe there's something in the rulebook about this I wasn't told. Like maybe labor relation troubles, a recession, shifting national politics, or some other concoction. But as is its just a gimmick. Overall I enjoyed the game, and I'd be willing to play it again. Though for the reasons I've stated I can't bring myself to rate it more than "6". 

Side note, with five players and at least two of them constantly sending air strikes against others I could see the game going on for quite a long time. For everyone's research would grind to a halt and you'd spend all your time doing repair.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a really solid worker-placement game.  However, in an abundant field of really solid worker-placement games, it fails for me to really stand out as being especially spectacular.  Probably my favorite thing about it is that the art is incredibly well done both in terms of visual appeal and functionality.  The iconography on the board and cards is very simple to understand and makes learning/teaching the game so much easier.  I also like that for each available action on the board, there are multiple spaces available with varying costs/payout - very clever.  I like the Espionage action, but I'm not overly fond of the bombing/repair aspect.  It's often more profitable to follow the repair action than take it directly, so I feel like using my one board action to repair is often wasteful and in many cases it can be cheaper to just build a new building.  The separate turns for placing and picking up is interesting in theory.  However, while it does provide for some timing and blocking planning, it's just doesn't feel as well executed in play as other games which use this such as Tzolk'in.  During much of the game I have enough buildings to use most or all of my workers, so spending one turn placing all of them, then the next turn picking up just feels a bit awkward.  Using my different worker types and buildings to chain cool combos together is very enjoyable, but if I'm able to efficiently use all of my workers in one turn, then I spend half the game just picking them up again.  The building costs are also inconsistent in that two buildings which provide the same output can have higher/lower input costs -- seeing an identical "cheaper" building come out just after you bought one is frustrating.  The deck should be staged so better buildings come out later in the game.  I do really appreciate the play length.  The play time on the box is often wildly optimistic but my few plays of this, even with a full 4 or 5 players, I've been able to teach and play in about 90 minutes, which is great.  Unfortunately, the game end often feels a bit sudden and unfulfilling.  One player finally builds a big bomb that gets them over the limit and that's it - game over.  Overall, it's a good game and I can see the appeal, but after a few plays I feel mostly done with it.  I can't imagine each game-to-game play feeling much different from this point.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Just when I thought I had all the worker placement games I could want along comes this gem. Mechanically, there's not a lot that's innovative and game-play will feel familiar. BGG folk are drawing analogies to Caylus. But I think it's more like Puerto Rico blended some of the worker placement of Caylus. There are also elements that remind me of Flying Buffalo Inc's Nuclear War. That aside, there are some nice differences (like blocking worker actions on the main board and bomber attacks against other players). The theme is well integrated but the mechanics are definitely Euro-esque. Adding the Nations card expansion will appeal to players who are craving something a little less Euro-like. I'm starting to appreciate direct player interaction again so this one gets high marks for venturing into that territory, along with an excellent use of theme. Player boards seem a bit of an afterthought compared to the rest of the components. I'm not a fan of the uranium/plutonium resource track. We've got bits for yellowcake, why not uranium and plutonium? But all in all, a solid release.

RATING: 8
After one play (5 players):

I really like this game. I feel it's incredibly well balanced. The reason I rate it so (relatively) low is because of the anticlimactic ending. There is little luck, almost everything is openly available to all players. It's a battle of balance and highly strategic. I've never played the revered Caylus but my search for WP games is sated for now!

*edit* I think changing it so that even in 4/5 player games the score is 60, this may make the game that much more enjoyable as it doesn't take one or two cards to all of a sudden declare a winner. The guy who won had tested a plutonium bomb (and got 6 pts), then flipped over and built a plutonium bomb that was 34 pts (now at 40), then loaded it (45). Game over. Noone saw that coming. Playing to 60 would have ramped up the tension. With this variant, may up the score.

*July 2012* Playing it more, there are various strategies available and multiple approaches. Not an easy game to win. Amazing design, I can't imagine why anyone would want to play another worker placement game again after playing this (Though Glenn Drover's Age of Empires intrigues me)

*Aug 2013* First play in a very long time. Used Nations expansion and the role cards from Second Stage. Even though the 4p game wrapped up in 2 hours, it felt like it goes on a bit too long. Despite this, a tight and well designed game.

RATING: 9
For me, a worker placement with a fabulous theme and flavor.  A nuclear arms-race where you need to build your "engine" and plan ahead, but also where tactics play a big part.  Timing of your placement/withdrawal of workers is critical, as your opponents can use you to their advantage if you're not careful.  
Speaking of opponent-interaction, I think this game does a great job of balancing the amount and necessity of interaction.  Some people are afraid of this game because Oh noes! you can get blowed up!  But in my experience, I've had people attack me several times, and first of all, the attacker is wasting some resources and time to do so, and second, there are ways to work around the damage they caused.  so much so, that sometimes I don't even bother to repair; I just find a different way to do the things I want to, and can still end up winning.  So overall, it turns out to be well done, I think.  The only time I could see it being an issue is if people are really picking on someone because they're actually jerks, rather than because they're playing strategically in the game; then it could probably ruin the experience for people.
Otherwise, usually a very tight race with second place being a turn or two behind, LOVE the artwork and the workers especially.  One of my favorites.

RATING: 5
The Manhattan Project has a stylized, colourful art style that is very clear and rather adorable. The font is art deco, the building cards look like WWII recruitment posters, the bomb cards look like the Obama 'Hope' poster, and (best of all,) the workers look like Lego men!

tMP may be difficult introducing to new players as the game goals and paths to achieve them need thorough explanations, also the importance of constructing buildings and performing espionage should specifically be emphasized.

tMP is a flawed game and a few game tweaks may need to be made: Remove the Bribe feature. Instead make discarding the cheapest building of the Construction Track a custodial task done every time someone retrieves their workers. This will prevent the game from grinding to a halt as the Construction Track clogs with uninteresting buildings.

The main flaw with tMP is that players must do too many conversions to score victory points. Its a 6 step process!

RATING: 6.5
Gioco potenzialmente molto bello coperto da un'ambientazione politicamente scorretta ma simpatica. Il gioco è un classico piazzamento lavoratori, molto german, che costruisce tutta la sua meccanica sull'acquisizione di lavoratori, specilizzati e non, e strutture per riuscire a costruire delle bombe atomiche potenti raffinando uranio e plutonio. L'intrazione è presente con il piazzamento che mira a occupare gli ambienti e portarli via agli altri. L'autore ha anche pensato di aumentare l'interazione e lo scontro con una sorta di battaglia tra i giocatori sfruttando la forta aerea di caccia e bombardieri. Questa parte è la delusione del gioco che mi porta a valutarlo non troppo bene. Infatti le azioni militari di fastidio sono molto dispendiose, molto lente e rendono vano qualunque tentativo di attacco. In sostanza quindi trovo The Manhattan Project un buon prodotto ma che necessita di radicali house rules per permettere di utilizzarne tutte le potenzialità (azioni) disponibili altrimenti si rischia che le azioni incremento flotta, battaglia e riparazione non vengano mai utilizzate per tutta la partita.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I've come to the conclusion that one of the best strategies in this game is to have a lot of mediocre buildings. Pretty much the only board actions I try to take are espionage and construction (for free). Having mediocre buildings means you are rarely the primary target for espionage - which can be deadly - and having a lot of them means it's expensive to try and bomb you; and when they do, it's less effective.
-----
Stylistically, I feel that TMP most closely compares to [thing=18602][/thing], in that it is a low-luck, somewhat vicious worker placement game. TMP arguably has more variability, fresher and tighter theme, and more viciousness. Whereas 2-player Caylus can be very chess-like, TMP is closer to [thing=25613][/thing], in that optimal play will vary based on the available cards.

Mechanically, it shares a lot with [thing=26566][/thing], in that you have a set of buildings that is for your exclusive use, and you have multi-stage conversion workers into resources into victory points. TMP dispenses with the land plot auctions (which is Homesteaders only point of interactivity) and adds in a main board for common actions, with airstrikes and espionage to make it more interactive.

I feel it is superior to [thing=34635][/thing], [thing=39938][/thing], and [thing=24480][/thing] because in those games, a string of bad luck can put you behind. Apart from the variability of the card draft, there is no luck in TMP.
-----
The game is unique in that you spend all game building up this potential energy, which gets suddenly released in a paroxysm of frenzied bomb building. For most of the game scores are at 0. It's only in the last few turns where there is any scoring at all.
-----
[url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/719419/the-manhattan-project-now-i-am-become-death-the]My BGG review [/url]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[b]The Manhattan Project[/b] 

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]I'd never had an opportunity to play Manhattan Project and after playing was completely surprised to find out it is a tableau/engine building game that introduces an interesting twist to the worker placement genre. 

In an nutshell you are using three types of workers to buy/build buildings and then use those buildings to carry out various actions [i](hiring more workers, mining yellow cake, converting yellow cake into Uranium or Plutonium)[/i] which all leads to building atomic bombs which is how you score points and win the game. There is a lot more nuance to the game, but I want to focus on what sets The Manhattan Project apart from other WP games and that is how workers are deployed and returned to your supply. 

On your turn you can take one of two actions. First you can place a worker to the main board and take the associated action. Once you take that action you are free to activate as many of the buildings as you have built in your own tableau. This becomes the crux of the game as you are trying to build the best buildings to help create an efficient engine that you can keep running. The second thing you can do on your turn is recall all of your workers, with from the main board and your tableau of buildings. The decision to recall workers is extremely important because that is all you do on that turn and you have now opened up all of those buildings for other players to use; they can even use your buildings via the espionage action. 

The only other mechanic I want to touch on is the fighter/bomber mechanic. You can attack other players via the air raid action during which you can destroy other fighters with your fighters or use bombers to damage your opponents building(s). The damaged building becomes unusable until they take an action and spend money to repair it. This may sound harsh, but similar to a cold war, the threat of retaliation is always looming over you once you spend your resources in an attack. No one got bombed in our game although it should have happened since this is the only way to slow down someone who has built a superior engine and needs to be brought back to the pack. This will definitely be the most divisive aspect of the game for some players, personally it didn't bother me. 

Your enjoyment of The Manhattan project will largely depend on whether or not you enjoy tableau/engine building games that have an inherent race element to them. I really enjoyed my play and also went out and bought a copy, but then I found out about [thing=176734][/thing] which eliminates the take that part of the game and appears to offer a deeper game play experience. I would still recommend trying out Manhattan Project if you haven't played before; I'm looking forward to playing again.[/i]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Lots of good ideas in the game. Unfortunately, also lots of issues that should have been resolved.

Primary among them is the Espionage action, which is potentially so strong that players essentially have no choice but to jump on it if it's free on their turn. I don't like games that have obviously superior choices since it then feels overly scripted.

The Espionage action is also problematic in that it's a far more annoying negative play experience then most things are in high-interaction games. Not only do you not get to use your stuff - somebody else gets to use it instead. At no penalty. The combination of choking off your own engine while supercharging someone else's leads to dramatically differing momentum trajectories that usually end up being insurmountable (assuming that the leader is competent).

I've played twice and am not particularly interested in playing again until I read commentary from gamers I respect that can prove that comebacks are possible and that the balance issues I see aren't there.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

特色是工人連動，所以如何規劃好有效使用建築物就很重要
造炸彈的分數range較廣
戰鬥機互炸的規則2人也比較沒什麼意思
可能相對適合多人的策略遊戲
規則:
*勝利條件為先達到特定分數 70-60-50-45 for 2-3-4-5 player
*根據人數挑選試爆版塊 06-048-0246-all，洗勻炸彈卡，開人數+1張在場上
*起始每人放token在間諜軌 鈾跟鈽軌，每個人拿四個基本工人，10元，戰鬥機跟轟炸機都在1，所有資訊都是公開的(炸彈卡隱藏)，根據順位獲得金錢或工人補償，0-2-4-2+一個工程師或研究員-4+一個工程師或研究員(顏色自選)
*每輪動作二選一
1.放工人 可以先放一個在主圖板上，接下來可以再放任意個在個人建築物上，也可做任意個炸彈行動
*主圖版除建築區外，先搶先贏，自己的建築也是先搶先贏，破壞的建築物不能使用
*可以故意放工人不做事情來卡格(自己建築物還是要放對應的工人)
*採到的格子有錢記號的，從供應區放一塊錢在左上角的煙灰缸
*循環標記代表所有其他玩家獲得的好處
*個人圖版的建築沒有上限，買完建築就全部往左移，開新的，蓋兩元的可以拿煙灰缸的所有錢
*空襲格 可同時攻擊多個玩家，減少一架戰鬥機，對手要減少一架飛機(攻擊者選)，如果對手沒有戰鬥機可減少一架轟炸機，放一個破壞token在目標建築物上
*修理一次可移三個破壞token
*設計炸彈 執行的玩家秘密挑一張，之後輪流拿，最後執行的玩家會有兩張
*間諜行動 提升間諜軌一格，此回合可以使用間諜軌個數的別人建築物，還是要放對應工人
*建炸彈行動 支付對應資源跟工人從手牌放出獲得分數> 鈽炸彈可選擇試爆，該卡放回牌庫底，拿目前最高分的試爆token(一場遊戲一次)，工人放上面(之後鈽彈記高分區)>可支付轟炸機加對應金錢裝在炸彈獲得5分
2.收工人 從主圖版跟其他玩家版拿回自己顏色工人 > 個人區所有使用中的工人回各自區域(中立回供應區，各自玩家的回手) > 主圖版跟個人區的中立工人回供應區

RATING: 7
Rated after one play. Medium complexity worker placement game in which the timing of when you remove your workers, taking into account everybody else's available resources, seems very important. Considerable player interaction (for a worker placement game) including a take that element and the ability to gang up on a perceived leader. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A stylish worker placement game with a touch of smack to whack the leader. Which unfortunately does invoke a sense of kingmaking which may cause the rating to drop. But the theme is turned into a fun one which can be easily hammed up in a strangelove type way, helped by the super artwork and a sensational board design. The rules are well presented and the game is easy to teach as well. Essentially you're creating an engine to generate plutonium or enriched uranium in order to build bombs for VPs. To get those you need an engine to build yellowcake and money. To get those, you'll need more workers than what you start with, so acquire engineers and scientists. You can only place one worker to the board each turn, so the key is to build buildings on your own board which drive your engine, because you can place on as many of your own buildings as you want in a turn. There's a spy option to place workers on other people's buildings, but it seemed too expensive and too prone to be non-useful (because the buildings you wanted were already claimed). And of course there's the smack, where you build up fighters and bombers and cause damage to other players' buildings. All that means though is that the non-targeted players get to concentrate on building more stuff instead of wasting actions on repairs (or generating fighters in the first place). So I'm not quite sure those two elements are right, but the rest provide a fast game with quick-enough turns where there are always options to progress. While each game provides the same engine building crux, at least each will provide a bit of difference due to the buildings on offer and which ones you obtain. Whether that's enough difference, not sure. I won't mind finding out though.

RATING: 7
One of my favourite worker placement games, although I’m not very good at it. At its heart it is a race game, the first player to build enough nukes that gets them to a set number of points wins. That sounds easy, but you can chain multiple workers together to have big turns so potential combinations are very important, couple that with the opportunity to use spies that will allow you to use other player’s buildings makes for some brain burning decisions.

I love the theme as well and the different kinds of workers (labourers, scientists and engineers) really help to bring the theme alive. This one is definitely a keeper.

RATING: 7
This is a worker placement game where you're trying to create a card-based engine to generate and process materials for nuclear weapons.  What's neat about this game is that you draft cards to your personal board, and while you can only use generic actions once per turn, you can use all of your personal actions at the same time.  Definitely an efficiency feel here.  However, the game regulates snowballing with direct conflict, which I'm not a big fan of.  Also, actually finishing the process of completing a bomb is very difficult; some players may not even have one by the time the game is over!  (I'm not sure that plutonium bombs are even worthwhile given the length of the game.)  For lack of a better word, I'd say that the scoring is too "jerky".

(2 plays)

RATING: 4

After playing the game a bit more and with a full complement of players, I would have to say that I'm enjoying it more. I'll be keeping this game just because Jen likes it and it plays 5 smoothly. Slightly worried about a runaway leader problem and how effective the planes actually are.

RATING: 7

+Second Stage & Nations expansions
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
This game became a quick hit with our group.

It's a worker placement game, but has some twists that keep it from feeling like a half-dozen other games.  Integration of gameplay and theme is excellent.  It feels like you are doing the things you'd have to in order to build atomic bombs.

Replayability is excellent so far.  There are always tough decisions to make, but not so many options that analysis paralysis is likely.  And we've still barely scratched the surface of the espionage and air strike options.

Random things I like:
- Really nice art direction.  I particularly like the look of the little Worker/Scientist/Engineer dudes (Red Scientist looks like Dr. Zoidberg, and Contractor Scientist looks like Atomic Robo!)  
- Putting the workers on really thick cardboard was an inspired decision.  The worker pieces feel like they have real "heft" to them, but (presumably) aren't all *that* more expensive than more standard counters like the fighter/bomber pieces. 

I do have a few minor quibbles:
- The "play to x points" victory condition works well enough for game balance, but doesn't make a lot of sense thematically.  One would tend to assume the *first* bomb would be of greater importance, (perhaps overwhelming importance, going by history).  The implicit historical background isn't spelled out, btw, but seems to be something like a 1940s Cold War, in a world without Hitler. 
- Anyway, it feels a little weird that being the world's first atomic power isn't necessarily all that big a deal, and that you can't actually *use* your atomic bombs.
- The "Nations" expansion really should be included with the main game.  It's such a tiny expansion, but great for adding flavor and replayability with the different national abilities.
- The cards are well-designed and attractive, but there's really not much reason they have to be so small, IMO.  Something like standard playing card size or a bit smaller would work a bit better.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
What fun twist on worker placement! My game group doesn't usually go for the super confrontational games, but we all agree this was delightfully vicious. Bombing other player's facilities is a necessary evil to keep them from sneaking in the win.

Watching your opponent's carefully is key and knowing when to recall all of your workers can be HUGE. Sometimes you want to take less actions on your turn so you can keep certain spots occupied. 

Overall: This was an instant favorite. Finally a worker placement where you can stop the leader dead in their tracks. I could see this game getting really nasty with certain players, but with players who have a method to their madness this will be a...BLAST!

RATING: 7
Excellent, smooth and relatively interactive worker placement game - but with one big problem.

The problem is that the bombing/airstrike part of the game is broken. It always seems to result in one player being bombed almost back to zero, never to recover. I need to discover whether the game's expansion pack fixes that problem, but it doesn't look like it will. Some players will argue that buying bombers means being distracted from one's victory conditions (thus 'kingmaking' by allowing a third or fourth player to win), or that various other interactions prevent the problem, but the reality is that the game makes it incredibly easy for certain players to build a big fleet of fighters/bombers (and actually pushes you towards it).

Apart from that problem, it's basically a more 'gotcha!' version of another good game - Viticulture (which seems more balanced).

RATING: 6
4-player game, preferably with players who know how to play this "correctly". I mean, you can ruin somebody's game if you want to do it, but you'll probably ruin your own game in the process.

I don't like the attacking in this game. It gets personal pretty quickly and feels unnecessary cruel. I also hate the fact that spreading the damage is usually quite hard(you probably don't have enough fighters) and it is better to hit one player with all your power.

Scales badly. Not a single rule change that would block some of the action spaces when playing with three players. Overall I'm not convinced the game is especially deep either, compared to many other WP-games.

RATING: 8
This is one of the first worker placement games I played that features two mechanics I really love:

 - Different worker types:  It works well here, with the Engineers and Scientists having different benefits and also the distinction of contract workers from the workers in each player's owned pool.  

 - Building new worker slots: The game has you building structures often, with each being a new slot for a worker to go.  The fact that you place these on a personal board and play mostly on your own board also introduces the feeling of engine-building to the game.

The theme is very cool.  I would like a little bit more variability in the structures, but this game definitely works well and is enjoyed by most people in my experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The Manhattan Project has extremely strong competition in the worker placement category. It does quite well, but is missing some little spark to set it apart from the competition. I don't know exactly what it is, but the end feels a bit vague and anticlimactic. Points are by and large awarded in such big chunks that the winning move feels like it's caught the other players unaware and has just told to them that all their plans and work has been for naught. They might be one round off the win, but the points total might not reflect this. Not that I'd have liked this to be a Feld-like mini points marathon. The five points for loading bombs is a nice touch that seems to indicate thorough play testing.

I like the aggression interaction they've come up with here. From annoying espionage to bombing buildings, for me it's the right level of playful in your face, messing with plans fun. One thing you'll learn in your first game is that it's NOT a good idea to buy the best buildings. They just become targets for espionage. Mediocre is best and I quite like this aspect. In most games you think of mediocre cards as being... well... mediocre, but here they're nice safe boring workhorses. It's quite different to other games.

There's plenty of good choices on your turn. The bombs all feel a bit too similar, and picking the right combination to get to your points goal is quite frankly a bit ho-hum. The placement and removal timing however works very well in terms of blocking and planning ahead with alternative moves. It's perhaps a bit too easy to get up to your full compliment of workers, but having so many to play around with certainly helps keep the pace of the game up.

I'm thinking three is the best number of players for this for my taste. The points total is high enough that a selection of maybe three bombs is needed, and the pace is high and not too chaotic. Four is okay with quicker players. I don't think five is the best experience.

Overall I like it and would suggest it over Caylus and several other of the top contenders in this category. It's got a quick pace, a novel theme and a nice level of player interaction.

RATING: 8
Worker placement games are all about to get things or actions to get other things that give players points. This is no different, but it has some new twists. First, players are able to construct buildings that give them abbilities different from their rivals. Second, players are able to attack other players to make damage to their buildings, so they can't be used. Third, players are able to spy on other players to use their buildings. Fourth, the workers are not automatic returned to the players; a has to be spendes to get them back. Fifth, there are workers (enginners and sciencetist) with special abbilities. The game is open until the end and players can feel the tension of a race. One of the best of the genre.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Received in trade, Dec. 2014.
First play didn't blow me away, but I also think certain aspects of the design are very clever.  Will probably hold on to it and try to get it to the table a couple times a year.

Second play (2-player):  This is a thematic game with some fun elements, but overall I just can't get into it wholeheartedly.  I think the primary issues are:
1)  Too long.  It just feels like it drags and gets repetitive in the latter half, rather than building towards some climactic finale (building bombs is actually not that satisfying).
2)  The primary attack element (airstrikes) are kinda fun, but their effect is to slow the game down further - and emotionally frustrate the targeted player.
3)  I just don't think I like the timing element of the game: spend a turn or two placing workers, spend a turn picking them all up.  It feels different and clever at first, but becomes stale quickly.  I prefer other worker placement games to this one.

RATING: 6
Played once with 3P. The Manhattan Project is a worker placement game with the unconventional theme of racing to build nuclear bombs. Much like in [thing=126163][/thing], taking back workers is an action on its own. This creates an interesting tempo in placing workers and retrieving them, and players generally want to position themselves to take advantage of the freed up slots when their opponents recall their workers. 

The first innovative aspect of The Manhattan Project is the Espionage action, which allows players to use their opponent's buildings and encourages players to take advantage of an opponent's free board. This somehow ties in with the fact that there are plenty of buildings which have 'strictly better' versions (which is a really questionable design choice on its own). Even if your opponent manages to get the better buildings, using Espionage makes those buildings available to you. But I also feel that the incentives to use Espionage are a bit [i]too[/i] strong, to the point where it is a no-brainer choice when the slot is open.

The second innovative feature is the ability to bomb your opponents' key buildings, which not only handicaps your target but also affects other players by shutting those buildings off to further Espionage actions. However, aggression doesn't really serve a purpose in this game. If used to rein in a leader, it has the typical problem of 'belling the cat' as the player who is most aggressive allows the other players a free ride. On the other end, it can quickly devolve into a prolonged war of attrition. Either way, I don't feel targeted conflict fits in a game of managing tempo and it drags the game on longer than needed. 

Speaking of which, the game feels a little long even without the bombing aspect. There are plenty of options on your personal board and often just as many workers, leading to a surprisingly time-consuming affair to chain all those actions together. But while it sounds as if the game is a mess, it does have its redeeming qualities. With the exception of the Espionage action, the decisions in this game are never obvious and the theme is pulled off well. I think this game needs a little more polish in the design (and possibly scrapping the bombing run mechanism) to be a real contender among worker placement games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
It is a really cool concept.  Unfortunately, this doesn't feel developed, not surprising from a Kickstarter project.  It suffers from a massive run-away leader problem, it effectively eliminates players and leaves them with little to do, it expects players to debilitate their defenses by trying to stop a runaway leader, and the deck large and is shuffled together entirely so outside of the set there is nothing remotely close to a planned dispersion of card types with no way to prevent clumping of essential cards.  

Three players really doesn't work, as the runaway leader can flood the guy to his right, so that they have to clear their board to do anything giving the workers back to the leader again.  This forces the other player to insure that they can get the espionage, which is not always possible, especially with the player to his right always reassembling.  Worse than that is that one player is expected to take the hit in efficiency in order to pound on the leader while the other player can just glide by without having to be responsible for it.  

Plus, pounding the leader games tend to suck, but at least most of them are in fillers that end in 20 minutes.  This takes hours.

On top of all that, it really is yet another worker placement euro, with nothing particularly special about it other than the theme.  If it were the case where the game was tight that might be enough, but everything is so half baked here.

******THIS GOT THE BONE*******

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I'm a sucker for worker placement games, especially ones that do something new.  While this game doesn't do much new it at least feels very new.

The theme is we are all playing nations building bombs and we are trying to put together the largest and most valuable arsenal, and the theme is pretty prevalent throughout the game with art that really evokes the WWII-era time period.  I especially love the newspaper-style rulebook.

The game itself is reasonably simple to teach and play.  It is done in much the same style as Tzolk'in where each turn you can place or retrieve workers.  The spaces are all self-explanatory and there are a lot of options and elements to the game.  You can build your own spaces, accumulate resources, design bombs to build, test, and load onto bombers for more points, and there's even a decent bit of interaction with the air strike and espionage mechanics.

My first game I definitely feel like it should have played faster than it did.  There were 3 of us and we had to cut it short to include some other people in a game but when we ended I feel like we were all on the cusp of ramping up to the end game (and there was a bit of AP going on to get there as it was all of our first game).  Once you accumulate enough buildings and workers you can get bombs out with decent regularity I think and the balance of the actions seems really fine-tuned.

It's also worth noting that the components are of the highest quality.  The worker tokens are so thick that when I took them out of the game box I thought they were two stuck together.

RATING: 7.5
It's a crime that WGN cancelled its surprisingly excellent "Manhattan" after two seasons, but I'll always be grateful for the added flavour it lends to our plays of this game. Instead of bringing dry recollections of facts that happened to other people to the table, we can instead intertextualise* vividly and shout phrases like "The implosion model works!" in our best Frank Winter voices.

I like TMP as a game for its themey little components (scientist meeples!) and for having lots of options which fluctuate based on other people's actions. I'm constantly diverted, and it's one of those cases where always having several possible movies is inspiring, not paralyzing.

I suspect that others wouldn't be too impressed with how my husband and I play, though. We simply have zero interest in bombing each other, preferring to take pride in our race for scientific supremacy, hoping that preparation is enough and violence won't be necessary. Maybe that's WGN's fault. They should give us a season 3 and let us find out.

(*I must bless the Australian Curriculum for deeming television shows and games as suitable texts to be studied in the English classroom, even if we do have to attach poncy words like "intertextualise" to the experience.)

RATING: 4
I wanted to love this game so much but was greatly disappointed. This is an unfinished game that is lacking in structure. It needs more play testing and many additional and modified rules to become a real game.

There is potential here but the amount of house rules We think are needed would make it into a completely different game. 

This is not a worker placement game where blocking of actions is a major part of the game. You don't even need to place workers on the main board! Instead it is a completely solo free form engine building race to points. The so called player screw-age is tacked on and not effective within the games economy. Although the espionage is possible if you are the only one focused on using it all the time. The points to win are to low and makes for a game that feels like it was shortened just to appeal to the masses that buy shorter games. The random draw of buildings, with no way to cycle, and bombs determine the winner more than the players do.

The most disappointing game experience I have ever had. I have a hard time understanding why this is so liked. Are these people that just enjoy doing an activity that feels like they are playing a game?

Update: It seams the first printing I played had some miss marked buildings that change the starting setup and we had some minor rules wrong. I don't believe these will change my opinion but I would play it again to see.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
An engine building game in which you race to build your bombs. Don't expect to keep your engine going for long. Once you get it going, the end of the game is not far. 

Choose your cards carefully, don't make yourself a target, keep an eye out for what the other players are doing, and try to time retrieving your workers to avoid being the victim of espionnage.

Use the airstrike sparingly if you don't want to leave yourself open to backstabbing, but it can provide a great opportunity to slow down your opponents.

You have lots of option every turn, but the best spots quickly disappear so you will have some tough decisions to make. Might be prone to AP with some players.

The rules are really easy to understand, the icons are clear and intuitive, and most people won't have trouble playing this game.

The game is beautiful to behold, with great art, and really functional. It is easy to see what is going on.

In the French version, all the expansions are included (great!) but there is no insert and no baggies (not great!), which can be problematic since there is a lot a cards and tokens in this game. My solution is a combination of pill bottles and baggies.

RATING: 8
Culled - First wave; Gifted to Grim

Why we culled it: This really is a wonderful worker-placement game, and the iconography for the workers is absolutely adorable. The game plays a lot like Agricola - players each have their own play mat that they can develop as well as a common board with actions that can be blocked or exploited until recall. The primary differences are (1) TMP features direct ways to interact with your opponents through the use of fighters/bombers to destroy buildings on opposing player boards and an espionage track to actually exploit their boards with your workers; and (2) the game length is open-ended and victory is instead determined by victory points (with a threshold to trigger the end game). While somewhat controversial with the theme of cold-war-era nuclear arms race - scoring points for building the most threatening arsenal (you don't actually use the nukes) - it wears the flavor well while remaining somewhat light in the presentation. All in all, it is a wonderful game. When it came time to cull our collection, this was one of the harder choices, but due to its overlap with Agricola and the wife-opposed existence of the espionage track, I decided to let it go to a good home that I know will allow me regular access to sessions when we want one.

RATING: 9
My first Worker Placement game, being one of the few out there that has a theme that even remotely appealed to me. I love anything and everything to do with nuclear weapons and history, so... I had to have this, no matter what kind of game it was. I collect little yellow cubes of YELLOW CAKE and refine them into plutonium, you say? I'm in.

But it's also a really awesome GAME and excellent (I think) representation of the worker placement genre. Strategic and tactical choices are there and I like the element of blocking and directly messing with other players' strategies (spies can use their private buildings that they paid to build and bombers can attack and destroy said buildings, or you can just use a space you know someone else needs because workers stay in place until someone passes ending a round.... all very cool).

Production and components are top notch and the art, while cartoonish, has a mature-kind-of-cuteness to it. Yeah, I said that. It's a thing. Think: the Android logo cute, not anime cute. Quirky, quaint, a tad silly, but fun. I can get on board (ha ha) with that.

RATING: 6.5
[thing=63628][/thing]... well, unimpressed me. The game has an interesting dynamic: you can make [u]one[/u] action on the main board and, afterwards, as many as you can/want on your own board - it is necessary to pay the activation costs. On the main board it is possible to build things, train engineers and scientists or get workers, you can enrich uranium or plutonium, send spies into other players buildings, enhance defenses or prepare more planes to attack, bomb other players buildings, and so forth.

In your own board you can do basically the same things (provide you have the buildings), but usually better - for example: you can get an Univesity, an it can provide you with 3 scientists when activated, as in the main board you could only get one.

All this is for one purpose: to be able to make atomic bombs. They can had a core made of plutonium or uranium, this last type is costier to get, but the bombs are worth usually more points. The player can also test its bombs, and then the next ones can be worth more points (many bombs has two stats: one without tests, other, higher, with a test made by the player). The bomb can be also be made to be carried by airplanes (it cost money, but this adds 5 points to the bomb).

All in all, is works rather well. But the points that the bombs are worth seem... excessive. Two bombs and one test might be all that you need to end game. Ok, make bombs isn't an easy task, but the end game can come almost abruptly: someone has, say, 20 points. The game ends with 50. One might think: hey, he still has to get 60% of the points necessary to end the game, so... Done, bomb, +30 points, end game.

The end seemed abrupt at best, and the points in the end (of my first game) were everything but close: 50, 35, 4 and 0.

I could be persuade to play [thing=63628][/thing], but I won't be asking for it.

Watch out for other countries fighters and bombers because if you get bombed, it's tough to repair the damage.

RATING: 10
Global Power Struggle Begins
Which nation will take the lead and become world's dominant superpower?

The Manhattan Project makes you the leader of a great nation's atomic weapons program in a deadly race to build bigger and better bombs. You must assign your workers to multiple projects: building your bomb-making infrastructure, expending your military to protect it, or sending your spies to steal your rival's hard work!

You alone control your nation's destiny. You choose when to send out your workers–and when to call them back. Careful managment and superior strategy will determine the winner of this struggle. So take charge and secure your nation's future!

Additional description:
The Manhattan Project is a low-luck, mostly open information efficiency game in which players compete to build and operate the most effective atomic bomb program. Players do not "nuke" each other, but conventional air strikes are allowed against facilities.
The game features worker placement with a twist; There are no rounds and no end-of-round administration. Players retrieve their workers when they choose to or are forced to (by running out).
An espionage action allows a player to activate and block an opponent's building, representing technology theft and sabotage.

RATING: 9
What's not to love about the idea of mass producing atomic bombs?  I have just one play through, but loved it.  It is a tough worker placement game, figuring out how to get your production line going, keeping up with the Jones' so you don't lose the arms race, and wondering whether you should be engaged in strategic bombing to keep the other guy down.  My group did not try either airstrikes nor espionage, and the game still went two hours with 3 players.  I enjoyed it and am eager to play again soon.  Just the base game.  Neither expansion looks that good to me right now.

Update: played it with the Nations expansion, and I took France.  France might be overpowered in a two player game. . It gave me access to bomb designs any time I put a scientist on the card, which is a huge advantage.  No draft required.  This can also allow you to decide very early which why you want your nuclear program to go, uranium or plutonium.  If you think money is tight, it's not in a 2 player game.  The opportunities to get cash are manifold, and that can really change the gameplay as well.

RATING: 8
Very interesting pacing for a worker placement game, both in the micro and macro sense. Micro: since you need to spend a turn to retrieve your workers, you often alternate between extremely eventful turns and very quick, uneventful ones. Macro: 80% of the game is getting the resources and infrastructure and workforce to make bombs, with your score between 0 and 10. Then at some point you just dump everything and suddenly bombs everywhere and someone hits 50 and wins.

RATING: 6
This is a fairly typical worker placement that stands out by the way that players can create their own little efficiency tableau in front of them in addition to picking stuff from the main board.  Players can only visit the main board once per turn, but can do as much as their workers allow on their own boards.  The key seems to be in making a nice efficiency engine in their own tableaus to exploit.  This isn't necessarily new (Belfort does it, too), but it's possible for other players to slow down that engine with bombing runs that can damage their buildings, which is something I haven't seen in the other games.  It's a little frustrating to be on the receiving end of it, but it's understandable, since without it a player could get into an engine loop that would guarantee them an easy win.  It's intriguing and interesting, and one of the better Kickstarter efforts I've played so far.  

2 plays: 4 players

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The Manhattan Project has one of the best riffs I have seen on the worker placement mechanism. Allowing you to place multiple workers per action, as well as chaining those rewards gained from one placement into the next open up a ton of strategies. Also, the bombing rules and espionage actions that let you steal rewards from your opponent's buildings and tie-up their worker spaces add a tremendous amount of direct interaction between players, something games in this genre often lack. The game also delivers a lot more decision making than the rulebook would lead you to believe, as there is a definite ebb and flow to the game that makes the "when" of an action as important as the "what".

The only minor complaint, and we are talking microscopic here, is that the game ends far too abruptly. At some point in the game, someone will just declare that they win...with very little warning. I suppose if you memorize the bomb deck it might be easier to predict, but what fun is that?

My rating for this will probably move. I know it's a strong eight at the very least, probably a nine. If my game group gets very competitive over it, a ten isn't even out of the question. I'll see how it goes...

RATING: 5
Sorry to say this, but the lack of development of the game significantly detracts from what could be a very good game.

The game is thematic, and has a somewhat novel worker placement mechanic.

However, our game went on forever because the card distribution was bad and we were short on a very important resource.  This caused the game to go on for hours and ultimately devolve into a situation where all the leaders bombed each other and effectively, the fourth place player won.

Why did this occur?  Because the game wasn't fully developed.  A simple fix - splitting the deck into A/B/C decks and then stacking them - could have resolved this problem. 

In today's market for games, I just don't feel like I can highly rate a game that has this type of flaw that could have been fixed but wasn't.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Worker placement with a twist.  Each turn you either place workers or retrieve them, unlike other WP games where you place, do the actions, and retrieve in the same turn.  There's the typical contention for action spots, but in addition to placing a worker on the main board, you also play actions on your own personal board.

The theme is heavy - you're building nuclear bombs, and it is integral with the gameplay.  You never forget about the theme while retrieving Yellowcake, producing Uranium and Plutonium, designing and building bombs, and even loading them onto bombers.

The game works great for two players, but is better with 3-5.  It's fun, unpredictable, and can end quickly.

RATING: 4
We were taught that players could use their Espionage powers EVERY turn, which turned our game into complete nonsense.

Will rate and review after playing correctly.

[i]Update[/i]:

Hmmm, well. It turns out that I prefer nonsense to sense.

We had a second play of Manhattan Project, with the correct rules, and it wasn't nearly as interesting as our first session.  Our first (incorrect) play was broken, but at least it was enjoyable in its own chaotic and wacky way.

Playing correctly, one thing that can happen is that a player can get bombed into oblivion early in the game - with no real way to recover.  That doesn't fit my category of fun, but it may fit yours.

Also, being able to purchase good buildings cheap (especially in the early game) is REALLY important.  If you can snag a good building - or set of buildings - early on, you'll have a big advantage over the other players.

RATING: 3
[-]Do not "get" why this is so popular and highly rated?
It is a random race game to build an "engine" and not really a worker placement game.
Workers help, but after your "engine" is built you don't need to even get all your workers or diverge and build more buildings.

Point in fact, my group seems like the only people to not want to play this game after the random element decided the winner in all games played.
[b]Bold[/b] Denotes worker spaces on the main board.

1 player did not have all his workers, had 4 buildings and every turn could make his bombs or raise in either U or P track.
Got bombed but was able to [b]Repair[/b] AND when someone else used [b]Repair[/b], was able to pay to repair what he could not earlier. 

He won just because the building cards came up randomly in his favor.
Even he said he did not even try to win, it just happened when the cards were there. (he knew he would win when he stumbled upon the "engine" though)

[b]Espionage[/b] (Esp.) was not very useful since he waited till the [b]Esp.[/b] player was forced to return workers and then continued, or use the main board to raise his P & U. No one could "physically" stop him making bombs since his bombs are not actually in a location. (big issue, causes run-away leader)

Why spend your resources building bombers, fighter planes, or doing [b]Esp[/b]. when they can use [b]Repair[/b], AND pay to repair when someone else uses [b]Repair[/b] or concerning [b]Esp.[/b] wait till your forced to return workers?

Any game you are forced to try and stop a player from winning instead of focusing on playing your game, you lose in more than one way.

Only game this year I regret getting (actually trading for.) Wish I would have played before accepting trade... Lol. None of the reviews or vids showed the random run away element very well.[/-]

May have to play it again, with a different mindset...
From rahdo's runthrough we played the U&P track wrong

But that does not negate the absolute futility of attacking or the runaway leader result.
It just shows the game would have been longer... shudder!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Worker placement with some issues. The most notable aspect of MP is that players can buy buildings that mirror spots on the board, except that the buildings are "free" actions - free in the sense that players can deploy any number of workers to unoccupied buildings per turn whereas they may only deploy to one space on the board. With enough buildings the most efficient structure becomes deploy everyone one turn, recall everyone the next. This is especially relevant because reactors players can build far exceed the enrichment options present on the board. Thus, with this turn structure established, big mines and reactors are superior to small ones - why not throw down as many people as possible on deploy turns, as they all return when you recall workers? By extension then, big bombs are preferable, even before considering how they have more favorable Uranium / Plutonium to VP ratios. Espionage and bombing buildings are dopey methods of keeping the leader in check, and one becomes leader usually when the building row is favorable to him.

RATING: 7
I love the round-less, constant play worker placement action system in this game, and am sort of surprised that I haven't come across something similar before. It's also neat that you can use as many workers as you want on your own board in addition to the one action on the main board, and having to use an action to recall your workers leads to a very fast flowing game (at least until the end game where it slows a tad, given the myriad numbers of options).

This game may actually rise in rating, and I very much look forward to more opportunities to play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Worker placement can be a problematic mechanism in a board game. The term is often used to describe games that have little to do with worker placement and games using it are sometimes too simple, boring or repetitive. The Manhattan Project, however, is probably the most elegant worker placement design I have seen. There are relatively few components and player actions, but gameplay is much deeper than initial impression. The theme and art are lovely, and the rules are simple to remember. With newbies, score differences can be astronomical in the end, but learning from your mistakes makes you desire a rematch. Or ten. All in all, an impressive little game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game deserves way more love than it is receiving on BGG.  Compare it with Agricola:  It's not as 'heavy', but neither is it for simpletons, and there is almost no luck involved.  Furthermore, those who are bothered by the 'multi-player solitaire' aspect of Agricola, should really like the Manhattan Project, what with the ability to launch air strikes and spy on other players.

- some people complain that:  you have to spend a turn retrieving your workers, as opposed to retrieval being a part of every turn/round.
- but that's part of the reason this game is so good!  those who make this complaint are like the person who complains that in chess the bishop doesn't move like the queen.

- some people complain that:  there is no way to guard against air strikes or espionage.
- no.  first, to protect against air strikes you can build up your fighters and to protect against espionage, you can retrieve your workers at the right time.  second, you are free to launch your own air strikes or to do your own spying.

- some people complain that:  it's formulaic.
- are you sure that everyone who is playing knows the rules?!

- some people complain that:  newbies are as likely to win as veterans.
- just . . . no.

- some people complain that:  the game is over too quickly, viz., after the winner has built just two bombs.
- I get this one, but you can increase the points required for a win, without adversely affecting gameplay.  

Please play and rate.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
As of now, we haven't played this, but I look forward to it. The worker pieces are adorable and feel solid, the board looks amazing and it's so clever, we love the plane tracker being a runway, and the fact that there aren't actually rounds unlike every other worker placement game we've played.
UPDATE: everything about the look and theme of the game is exquisite

That last point is going to be strange to get used to, I think. In something like Lords of Waterdeep, you take turns placing one guy per turn until everyone has placed, then you all retrieve your guys at the end of the round. But here, you play one on the main board, and then as many as you are able to play on your personal board. You could go through a dozen guys in a turn, and run out, so you might have to spend a turn just retrieving all your guys. That isn't the round-end action, that's just your turn, and you immediately open up spaces for your opponents to fill.
UPDATE: the strategy is certainly different

We also like the prospect of things being acquired and usable in the same turn. Using a worker to buy a building that you can immediately put a worker on to gain a scientist that you can immediately put on another building to gain another output. Brilliant.
UPDATE: yup, it's pretty great to chain together

Bomb testing seems interestingly competitive. You design a bomb, do a card draft to get one you want to build, then build it when you have enough workers to assign to it, but then if it's a Plutonium bomb, it's only worth maximum points if you've already tested (wasted) a Plutonium bomb. And the later in the game you do your one and only test, the fewer points you get in return for that wasted bomb.
UPDATE: bomb testing so far irrelevant

RATING: 8.5
I like this interactive eurogame very much. The possibility to bombard your neighbours buildings is fun and interactive and espionage makes you pay attention to what your neighbour is doing. 
The theme is brilliant and the flavor is all there. I like how each game is very different. Sometimes no buildings of a certain type comes out of the deck and causes a bottleneck that makes you adapt your strategy. 
Some players mentioned the kingmaker issue caused by the possibility to bombard buildings. I experienced it's rather a part of the strategy in which you deliver a precision strike to slow down your opponents. 
I appreciate that the game is very flexible, you can play with two to five players without experiencing problems.
